Patients are frequently males in their 30s and 40s who are not inoculated with rubella vaccine as routine immunization, and the majority of patients have no or unknown history of rubella vaccination.\n\nRubella was epidemic in Japan in 2012-2013; more than 14 000 rubella patients and more than 40 congenital rubella syndrome patients were reported.\n\nRubella immunity should be confirmed before traveling to Japan.\n\nThe latest information about rubella in Japan is available from https://www.niid.go.jp/niid//images/idsc/disease/rubella/2018pdf/rube18-38.pdf.\n\n--\nSatoshi Kutsuna\nDisease Control and Prevention Center\nNational Centre for Global Health and Medicine\n<skutsuna@hosp.ncgm.go.jp>\n\n[The URL listed here has excellent figures in Japanese and English.\n\nRubella is a mild, contagious disease caused by the rubella virus, genus _Rubivirus_, family Togaviridae.\n\nThe National Institute of Infectious Diseases warned that a potential rubella outbreak could affect the 2020 games and is urging people -- mainly men in their 30s to 50s -- to get vaccinations, as many have not been vaccinated fully against the disease. The vaccine is given as part of a combination vaccine, called the MMR vaccine, that protects against measles, mumps, and rubella.\n\nJapan saw a major rubella outbreak in 2013, with over 10 000 people infected. Cases of infection have since declined, but there has been a resurgence since late July this year [2018], with many of the patients being men in their 30s and older. - Mod.LK\n\nHealthMap/ProMED map available at:\nJapan: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/156]", "summary": "\n\n--\nSatoshi Kutsuna\nDisease Control and Prevention Center\nNational Centre for Global Health and Medicine\n<skutsuna@hosp.ncgm.go.jp>\n\n[The URL listed here has excellent figures in Japanese and English. Symptoms include a rash and fever for 2-3 days. Rubella is very dangerous for a pregnant woman and her developing baby. If a pregnant woman gets rubella virus, her baby could have birth defects such as deafness, cataracts (blurred vision), heart defects, mental disabilities, and organ damage.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Overall, rubella cases have decreased in Spain from a peak in 2005 of 592 cases reported. The cases were reported in children and some adults. One death of a child was reported.\n\nThe Ain Issa camp, about 50 km [31 mi] north of Raqqa, includes about 3000 tents and is inhabited by more than 20 000 displaced people from Raqqa and Deir Al-Zour.\n\nRubella, or German measles, infection in people is typically mild. However, in pregnant women, rubella can cause death or birth defects such as cataracts, deafness, glaucoma, and heart, lung, and brain abnormalities in an unborn baby (fetus) during the 1st few months of pregnancy.\n\nRubella is a highly contagious disease. People who are infected with rubella are infectious from 1 week before to at least 4 days after the rash appears. It is transmitted through airborne droplet or close contact with the patient, but it is vaccine-preventable.\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Rubella is generally a mild disease in children; the primary medical danger of rubella is the infection of pregnant women because it can cause congenital rubella syndrome in developing babies. Before a vaccine against rubella became available in 1969, rubella epidemics happened every 6-9 years, usually among children 5-9 years old, along with many cases of congenital rubella. Thanks to immunization, there are far fewer cases of rubella and congenital rubella.\n\nMost rubella infections today appear in young, non-immunized adults rather than in kids. In fact, experts estimate that 10 per cent of young adults are currently susceptible to rubella, which could pose a danger to any children they might have someday.\n\nCongenital rubella syndrome can have potentially devastating consequences for the developing fetus. Children who are infected with rubella before birth are at risk for growth problems, intellectual disability, defects of the heart and eyes, deafness; and liver, spleen, and bone marrow problems (excerpted and edited from http://kidshealth.org/en/parents/german-measles.html).\n\nAccording to the WHO European office, 2.6 million Syrians fled their homes for safer parts of the country last year (2017). That's about 7700 displacements per day \u200a-- \u200aone of the highest rates in the world. In total, after almost 7 years of conflict, more than 6.1 million people have been uprooted inside the country.\n\nWhile rubella cases are not common, displaced Syrians had little access to health care while dislocated, and consequently children were not vaccinated. In partnership with donors like the United Kingdom's Department for International Development (DFID) and the Government of Norway, WHO has been supporting NGOs to provide health assistance in 7 camps in Raqqa and Hassakah governorates. In the last 3 months of 2017, 13 partners provided more than 216 000 medical consultations in camps, informal settlements and host communities across north eastern Syria.\n\nSyria Immunization Group (SIG), a confederation of health organizations coordinated by WHO, has been working to make sure children in war-torn northern Syria don't miss out on vaccinations. Despite airstrikes, sniper fire, and destroyed roads, SIG teams have managed to vaccinate millions of children against polio, measles, and other diseases in the past 2 years. - Mod LK\n\nMaps of Syria: http://www.lib.utexas.edu/maps/middle_east_and_asia/syria_admin_2007.jpg and http://healthmap.org/promed/p/63814]", "summary": "It is transmitted through airborne droplet or close contact with the patient, but it is vaccine-preventable.\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Rubella is generally a mild disease in children; the primary medical danger of rubella is the infection of pregnant women because it can cause congenital rubella syndrome in developing babies. As of [4 Sep 2019], there have been 2156 Rubella cases reported by the NIID during 2019.\n\nThis is an increase of about 260 rubella cases in Japan since July 2019. On a local basis, the city of Tokyo has reported 37% of Japan's 2019 Rubella cases.\n\nSince Rubella is very dangerous for a pregnant woman and her developing baby, the US Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) said on [7 Aug 2019], 'pregnant women who are not protected against rubella through either vaccination or previous rubella infection, should not travel to Japan during this outbreak.'\n\nBut, pregnant women should not get a Rubella vaccination with the measles-mumps-rubella (MMR) says the CDC. This is because the MMR vaccine is an attenuated 'live virus' vaccine.\n\nThe CDC says 'pregnant women who are not vaccinated should wait to get [the] MMR vaccine until after they have given birth. And, women of childbearing age should avoid getting pregnant for at least 4 weeks after receiving the MMR vaccine.'\n\nAdditionally, the CDC says 'if a pregnant woman contracts the rubella virus, her baby could have birth defects such as deafness, cataracts, heart defects, mental disabilities, and organ damage.'\n\nAnd, when a rubella infection occurs during early pregnancy, serious consequences, such as miscarriages, stillbirths, and severe birth defects in infants, which are known as Congenital Rubella Syndrome (CRS), [may result].\n\nThis new NIID report indicates there have been 3 CRS cases in Japan during 2019. As a comparison, during 2005-2015 in the USA, only 8 babies with CRS were reported.\n\nMoreover, [fewer] than 10 people in the USA are reported as having rubella each year. Since 2012, all rubella cases had evidence that they were infected when they were living or traveling outside the USA.\n\nTo alert international travelers, the CDC issued a Level 2 Travel Alert regarding Japan's ongoing Rubella virus outbreak in August 2019. This 'Practice Enhanced Precautions' Travel Alert says 'travelers to Japan should make sure they are vaccinated against rubella with the MMR vaccine before visiting Japan.' This CDC Travel Alert is important since approximately 4.5 million US citizens visit Japan annually.\n\nAdditionally, the Public Health Agency of Canada and the UK Foreign Travel Advice recommend 'pregnant women who are not protected against rubella avoid traveling to Japan.'\n\nIn the USA, there are 2 approved rubella vaccines: MMR II-Rubella and ProQuad. Both rubella vaccines are available at most pharmacies. Travelers to Japan can request a rubella vaccine counseling appointment with a local pharmacist.\n\nRubella vaccines, like any medicine, can produce side effects. [People] are encouraged to report vaccine side effects to a healthcare provider or the CDC.\n\n[Byline: Dani Reiter]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[See discussion of rubella in ProMED-mail Rubella - Japan (02) 20190811.6617360. - Mod.LK\n\nHealthMap/ProMED-mail map:\nJapan: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/156]", "summary": "\n\nSince Rubella is very dangerous for a pregnant woman and her developing baby, the US Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) said on [7 Aug 2019], 'pregnant women who are not protected against rubella through either vaccination or previous rubella infection, should not travel to Japan during this outbreak.' RUBELLA - JAPAN (03): SPREAD, TRAVEL ALERT\n******************************************\n As of [4 Sep 2019], there have been 2156 Rubella cases reported by the NIID during 2019.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["measles", "mumps", "rubella"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2019-07-25 00:00:00", "2019-08-25 00:00:00", "2019-03-25 00:00:00", "2012-03-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "USA", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Tokyo", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Canada", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Japan", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "US", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "6887119", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Rubella - Asia: Japan", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=6887119", "date": "2020-01-13 21:09:46", "main_text": "RUBELLA - ASIA: JAPAN\n**********************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n[There is a correction of this report: Rubella - Asia: Japan, corr 20200115.6889777\nThe text below erroneously gives an increase of 6288 reported cases in week 50 (from 2288 the prior week). The actual number of new cases was 6 with a cumulative total of 2294 cases for the week. - ProMED-mail regrets this error. - Mod.JH]\n\n[1]\nDate: Tue 7 Jan 2020\nSource: National Institute of Infectious Diseases Epidemiology Center for Infectious Diseases [in Japanese, trans. & summ. Rapp. KI, edited]\nhttps://www.niid.go.jp/niid/images/epi/rubella/2019/rubella191225.pdf\n\n\nUrgent Information on Rubella Outbreak in Japan: as of 25 Dec 2019\n------------------------------------------------------------------\nRubella reports in week 51 of 2019 [16 Dec-22 Dec 2019], 6 cases were diagnosed with rubella and reported.\n\nRubella in weeks 1-51\n---------------------\nThe cumulative number of reported cases was 2294, an increase of 6288 from 2288 in week 50 (Figures 1, 2-1, and 2-2). Even if diagnosed in the 51st week, reports reported late on or after [26 Dec 2019] are not included, so care must be taken in interpreting the number of reports.\n\nNumber of reported cases of congenital rubella syndrome\n-------------------------------------------------------\nThe number of cases of rubella and congenital rubella syndrome reported since the start of notification of all cases in 2008 (Figure 3), 2014 report. Since then, there have been no reports of congenital rubella syndrome\n(http://www.niid.go.jp/niid/ja/rubella-m-111/rubella-top/700-idsc/5072-rubella-crs-20141008.html)\n\nA total of 4 people were reported, 1 each in weeks 4, 17, 24, and 44 in 2019\n\nPresumed infected area: Saitama Prefecture 1 person, Tokyo 2 people, Osaka Prefecture 1 person.\n\nGender: 3 males, 1 female\n\nKuching vaccination history: Yes (1 time, vaccination year unknown, type unknown) 1 person, 3 unknown, maternal rubella history during pregnancy: Yes 1 person, unknown 2 people, none 1).\n\nRubella reports since 2013\n--------------------------\nSince the epidemic of 2013 (14 344), there were 319 in 2014, 163 in 2015, 126 in 2016 and 91 in 2017.\n\nDespite a downward trend (Figures 2-1, 2-2, 3), 2946 people were reported in 2018, and 2294 in week 51 in 2019.\n\nNumber of reports by region\n---------------------------\nBy region, Tokyo (854: no increase from week 50), Kanagawa (293: one increase from week 50), Yo-ken (200: no increase from week 50), Saitama (197: no increase from week 50), Osaka (130: no increase)\n\n(No increase from 49 weeks), with more than 100 reports (Figures 4 and 7). Week 51 is for Aichi prefecture (3 people) (Figure 5).\n\nThe largest number was 250 from the Kinki region (11%), 168 from the Kyushu region (7%), 125 from the Chubu region (5%), 96 people (4%) from the Chugoku / Shikoku region and 72 (3%) from the Hokkaido / Tohoku region were reported. No report is high. Only in Chichi Prefecture (Figures 4 and 7).\n\nSymptoms (with duplication)\n----------------------------\nIn the descending order, rash 2262 (99%), fever 2035 (89%), lymphadenopathy 1324 (58%), conjunctival congestion 1066 (46%), cough 567 (25%), arthralgia / arthritis 542 (24%), nasal discharge 499 (22%), thrombocytopenic purple 7 (0.3%) had plaque disease and 1 (0.04%) had encephalitis. In addition; sore throat 41; headache 42; malaise 24; 11 people, diarrhea / watery / soft stools; 11 people, papular hemorrhage of hard palate / palate mucosa; 8 people, thrombocytopenia; 7 people, leukopenia; 3 people, hepatitis / hepatic dysfunction; meningitis in 1 person, and pneumonia in 1 person. Fever, rash, lymphadenopathy\n\nAll reported were 1182 (52%).\n\nLaboratory diagnosis method (with duplication)\n----------------------------------------------\nVirus isolation was 26 (1%), 4 in 1E, and 2 in 2B. Detection of viral genes by PCR 1326. Of these (58%), of which 625 had been genotyped, 548 in 1E and 35 in 2B.\n\n1207 (53%) of serum IgM antibodies were detected, of which both viral genes and serum IgM antibodies were detected; were 413 (34%). Rubella antibody seroconversion or significant increase with paired sera was found in 54 (2%) patients.\n\nIn addition, after being accepted by the public health center as measles (clinical diagnosis example), the result of the test diagnosis is reported to rubella (test diagnosis example): there were 136 cases in which this was changed.\n\nPutative source of infection\n----------------------------\nProfession\nIn the occupation statement column added to the notification slip from January 2019, 837 (36%) were listed as company employees.\nThe most common was 33 healthcare professionals (10 nurses, 5 medical clerks, 4 pharmacies, 3 doctors, 2 occupational therapists, 2 nursing assistants, 1 dentist, 1 pharmacist, 1 dental assistant, working at a dental clinic 1 person, 1 laboratory technician, 2 medical professionals), 12 childcare workers, 16 teachers, 11 police officers and police officers, 7 firefighters were reported, and 7 SDF personnel were reported.\n\nAge and gender\n94% (2166) of the reported cases are adults, with 3.6 times more men than women (1795 men, 499 women) (Figure 8, 9, 10). The median age of male patients is 40 years (0-76 years), especially for men in their 30s and 40s (59% of all men) (Figure 8). The median age of female patients is 30 (0-76), especially in their 20s and 30s (64% of all women). Japan (02)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=6617360", "date": "2019-08-11 21:08:32", "main_text": "RUBELLA - JAPAN (02)\n********************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Sat 10 Aug 2019\nSource: Outbreak News Today [abridged, edited]\nhttp://outbreaknewstoday.com/rubella-in-japan-cases-top-2000-cdc-revisits-travel-notice-95847/\n\n\nThis week, the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) reviewed a travel notice last updated in March 2019 concerning travel to Japan and the rubella outbreak.\n\nTravelers to Japan should make sure they are vaccinated against rubella with the MMR (measles, mumps, and rubella) vaccine before travel, the federal health agency notes.\n\nAccording to the National Institute of Infectious Diseases in Tokyo, 2039 rubella cases have been reported through the end of July 2019, with most cases continuing to be reported in the Kanto region (Tokyo, Kanagawa, Chiba, and Saitama).\n\nRubella is a contagious disease caused by a virus. Most people who get rubella usually have a mild illness, with symptoms that can include a low-grade fever, sore throat, and a rash that starts on the face and spreads to the rest of the body.\n\nRubella can cause a miscarriage or serious birth defects in a developing baby if a woman is infected while she is pregnant. The best protection against rubella is MMR (measles-mumps-rubella) vaccine.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[During 2018, Japan reported a total of 2917 rubella cases. The initial CDC Travel Alert for Japan regarding this rubella outbreak was issued on 22 Oct 2018.\n\nThe Japan rubella epidemic is mainly affecting men from their late 30s to early 50s. This is because, between August 1977 and March 1995, the rubella vaccine was only given to girls.\n\nWith a change in vaccination policy, Japanese boys began receiving rubella vaccinations during April 1995.\n\nRubella on its own is not a high-risk infection, but rubella infection during early pregnancy can pose serious risks to the foetus, as in 90% of the cases, the virus is transmitted to the foetus. This can result in miscarriage, stillbirth, or congenital rubella syndrome (CRS) in infants. CRS can cause deafness, disabilities, heart impairment, autism, and other birth defects. Around 40 000 children are born with birth defects due to CRS every year in India, according to WHO. - Mod.LK\n\nHealthMap/ProMED map available at:\nJapan: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/156]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n Communicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[During 2018, Japan reported a total of 2917 rubella cases. \n\nRubella on its own is not a high-risk infection, but rubella infection during early pregnancy can pose serious risks to the foetus, as in 90% of the cases, the virus is transmitted to the foetus. \n\nTravelers to Japan should make sure they are vaccinated against rubella with the MMR (measles, mumps, and rubella) vaccine before travel, the federal health agency notes. \n\nRubella can cause a miscarriage or serious birth defects in a developing baby if a woman is infected while she is pregnant. Japan (02): alert, USA travel alert", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=6110667", "date": "2018-10-25 13:38:45", "main_text": "RUBELLA - JAPAN (02): ALERT, USA TRAVEL ALERT\n*********************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nIn this update:\n[1] Japan: more than 1100 cases\n[2], [3] US CDC: travel alert\n\n******\n[1] Japan: more than 1100 cases\nDate: Tue 16 Oct 2018\nSource: The Mainichi [edited]\nhttps://mainichi.jp/english/articles/20181016/p2g/00m/0dm/046000c\n\n\nMore than 1100 rubella cases have been reported this year [2018] in Japan, raising concern about serious health impacts on unborn babies who could be infected with the disease by their mothers during pregnancy, a national institute said [Tue 16 Oct 2018].\n\nThe 1103 cases so far in 2018 represent a nearly 12-fold jump from the entire previous year and also an increase of 135 in the 1st week of October [2018] from the previous week, according to the National Institute of Infectious Diseases.\n\nMothers with children born with congenital rubella syndrome in the past are calling on people to get vaccinations as infection in the early stages of pregnancy can cause birth defects such as hearing impairments, cataracts and heart disorders.\n\n'We need to keep monitoring the situation carefully to see if it will develop into a major epidemic,' health minister Takumi Nemoto said at a news conference. He also said the ministry will urge women who want to get pregnant to undergo a rubella antibody test.\n\nIn a 2012-2013 rubella epidemic in Japan, which saw more than 16 000 rubella patients, 45 babies developed congenital rubella syndrome and 11 of them died.\n\nKayo Kani, a 64-year-old co-head of a group working to stop rubella infections, warned that the latest epidemic could also result in the birth of babies with the syndrome, although no cases have been reported so far this year [2018].\n\n17 years ago, Kani lost her daughter who was born with heart problems due to the syndrome. Having learned that women of her daughter's generation are now at risk of giving birth to children with similar problems, Kani said, 'I feel like my daughter is telling me to eliminate rubella infections.'\n\nTo prevent serious health repercussions arising from the contagious disease, which is often transmitted through coughing and sneezing, vaccinating people around pregnant women is important, according to the institute.\n\nWomen who are already pregnant cannot be inoculated as the vaccine itself is feared to have an impact on the child. Notable rubella symptoms are fever, sore throat, and a rash. Its incubation period is 2-3 weeks.\n\nMany patients found this year [2018] have been men in their 30s to 50s, a group who has not been vaccinated sufficiently.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail Rapporteur Kunihiko Iizuka\n\n[The cumulative incidence this year (2018) is highest in Chiba prefecture (37.6 cases per million population), followed by Tokyo (32 cases per million population), and Kanagawa prefecture (17.9 cases per million population).\n\nIn the past 6 weeks, 914 cases were reported, making up most of the 1289 cases recorded this year as of last Wed 18 Oct 2018. In comparison, only 93, 126, and 163 cases were recorded in 2017, 2016, and 2015 respectively. - Mod.LK]\n\n******\n[2] US CDC: travel alert\nDate: Tue 23 Oct 2018\nSource: NHK World-Japan [edited]\nhttps://www3.nhk.or.jp/nhkworld/en/news/20181023_38/\n\n\nUS health officials have warned that pregnant women who are not protected against rubella, or German measles, through either vaccination or previous infection, should not travel to Japan during the current outbreak in the country.\n\nThe number of patients with the viral infection is increasing, mostly in Tokyo and neighboring prefectures.\n\nThe US Centers for Disease Control and Prevention on [Mon 22 Oct 2018] raised its advisory level for the rubella outbreak in Japan to 'alert,' the 2nd highest of 3 levels.\n\nThe health officials say that travelers to the country should make sure they are vaccinated against the infection before travel.\n\nBabies born to women who contract the disease during pregnancy are at risk of having birth defects, such as eye, ear, or heart disorders.\n\nThe World Health Organization announced in 2015 that the Americas are the world's 1st region to eliminate rubella and congenital rubella syndrome.\n\nJapan's health ministry says that at the moment, the US is the only country to call for pregnant women not to travel to Japan.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail Rapporteur Kunihiko Iizuka\n\n******\n[3] US CDC: travel alert\nDate: Mon 22 Oct 2018\nSource: Travelers' Health CDC [edited]\nhttps://wwwnc.cdc.gov/travel/notices/alert/rubella-japan\n\n\nRubella in Japan, alert - level 2, practice enhanced precautions\n----------------------------------------------------------------\nKey points\n- There is an outbreak of rubella in Japan.\n- Travelers to Japan should make sure they are vaccinated against rubella with the MMR (measles, mumps, and rubella) vaccine before travel.\n- Rubella is very dangerous for a pregnant woman and her developing baby. Pregnant women who are not protected against rubella through either vaccination or previous rubella infection should not travel to Japan during this outbreak.\n\nWhat is the current situation?\nHealth officials in Japan have reported an outbreak of rubella. Most cases continue to be reported in the Kanto region (Tokyo, Kanagawa, Chiba, and Saitama).\n\nWhat can travelers do to protect themselves?\n- Make sure you are fully vaccinated or otherwise protected against rubella.*\n- People who cannot show that they were vaccinated or are otherwise protected against rubella should get vaccinated before leaving the United States:\n- Infants (6-11 months of age) should have 1 dose of MMR (measles, mumps, and rubella) vaccine. Children in the United States routinely receive MMR vaccination at age 12-15 months.\n- Adults and children 1 year of age or older should have 2 doses of MMR vaccine separated by at least 28 days.\n- Avoid contact with people who are sick.\n*Those who are otherwise protected include people who were born before 1957 and those who have been tested and have confirmed immunity. Talk to a doctor to see if you are protected against rubella.\n\nSpecial advice for pregnant women\nWhen rubella infection occurs during early pregnancy, serious consequences -- such as miscarriages, stillbirths, and severe birth defects in infants, congenital rubella syndrome, CRS [https://www.cdc.gov/rubella/pregnancy.html] -- can result. See more about pregnancy and rubella at https://www.cdc.gov/rubella/pregnancy.html.\n\nPregnant women should\n- talk with their healthcare providers before traveling to Japan to check whether they are protected against rubella and whether it is advisable to travel;\n- avoid traveling to Japan during this outbreak if not protected against rubella, through either vaccination or previous rubella infection. This is especially important during the 1st 20 weeks of pregnancy;\n- get MMR vaccine after they have given birth, if they are not already protected against rubella.\n\nLearn more about preventing rubella and what to do if you think you have it on the rubella page for travelers [https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/travel/diseases/rubella.\n\nWhat can clinicians do?\n- Check that your patients 6 months of age or older who will be traveling internationally are protected against rubella.\n- Think Travel: keep rubella in mind when treating patients with fever and rash, especially if the patient has recently traveled internationally.\n\nTraveler information\n- Rubella webpage for travelers: https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/travel/diseases/rubella\n- CDC rubella homepage: https://www.cdc.gov/rubella/index.html\n- MMR (measles, mumps, rubella) vaccine information statement: https://www.cdc.gov/vaccines/hcp/vis/vis-statements/mmr.html\n- MMRV (measles, mumps, rubella, varicella) vaccine information statement: https://www.cdc.gov/vaccines/hcp/vis/vis-statements/mmrv.html\n\nClinician information\n- Rubella in CDC's Yellow Book (Health Information for International Travel): https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/travel/yellowbook/2018/infectious-diseases-related-to-travel/rubella\n- Advisory Committee on Immunization Practices (ACIP) Recommendations for MMR vaccine (http://www.cdc.gov/vaccines/hcp/acip-recs/vacc-specific/mmr.html) and MMRV vaccine (http://www.cdc.gov/vaccines/hcp/acip-recs/vacc-specific/mmrv.html)\n- Rubella information for healthcare professionals\n- Think Travel homepage https://www.cdc.gov/thinktravel/\n\nWhat is rubella?\nRubella, also called German measles, is a disease spread by the coughs and sneezes of infected people. Symptoms include rash and fever for 2-3 days. Rubella is very dangerous for a pregnant woman and her developing baby. If a pregnant woman gets rubella virus, her baby could have birth defects such as deafness, cataracts (blurred vision), heart defects, mental disabilities, and organ damage.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail Rapporteur Kunihiko Iizuka\n\n[Maps of Japan: http://mapsof.net/uploads/static-maps/cities_of_japan_map.png and http://healthmap.org/promed/p/156]", "summary": "https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/travel/diseases/rubella\n- CDC rubella homepage: https://www.cdc.gov/rubella/index.html\n- MMR (measles, mumps, rubella) vaccine information statement: https://www.cdc.gov/vaccines/hcp/vis/vis-statements/mmr.html\n- MMRV (measles, mumps, rubella, varicella) vaccine information statement: https://www.cdc.gov/vaccines/hcp/vis/vis-statements/mmrv.html\n\nClinician information\n- Rubella in CDC's Yellow Book (Health Information for International Travel): https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/travel/yellowbook/2018/infectious-diseases-related-to-travel/rubella\n- Advisory Committee on Immunization Practices (ACIP) Recommendations for MMR vaccine (http://www.cdc.gov/vaccines/hcp/acip-recs/vacc-specific/mmr.html) and MMRV vaccine (http://www.cdc.gov/vaccines/hcp/acip-recs/vacc-specific/mmrv.html)\n- Rubella information for healthcare professionals\n- Think Travel homepage https://www.cdc.gov/thinktravel/\n\n \n\nPregnant women should\n- talk with their healthcare providers before traveling to Japan to check whether they are protected against rubella and whether it is advisable to travel;\n- avoid traveling to Japan during this outbreak if not protected against rubella, through either vaccination or previous rubella infection. Japan\nDate: Sat 24 Nov 2018\nSource: Yahoo Japan [translated, edited]\nhttps://headlines.yahoo.co.jp/hl?a=20181124-00000015-mai-soci\n\n\nExcerpts:\nExpansion of the epidemic of rubella continues. The cumulative number of patients reported by 11 Nov this year [2018] is 2032, exceeding 2000 for the 1st time in 5 years since the epidemic in 2013, when more than 14 000 patients came out.\n\nGenerally, it takes about a year and a half to manufacture MR vaccines from scratch in Japan, and the vaccine expires one year after going to the market. According to the current Japanese system, it is difficult for vaccine manufacturers and distributors to keep stocks in large quantities, except for regular vaccinations for children, and there are hundreds of thousands of annually available stocks as voluntary inoculations and emergencies.\n\nSince October 2018, the country has asked manufacturers to add additional MR vaccines intensively to 5 prefectures in Tokyo, Chiba, Kanagawa, Saitama, and Aichi prefectures where rubella is prevalent.\n\nWomen who want to become pregnant and those cohabiting family of pregnant women are preferentially vaccinated in October.\n\nThe supply of MR vaccine and rubella alone vaccine is in short supply since the outbreak of measles in Okinawa Prefecture in March this year [2018].\n\nFor adults only, we recommend MMR vaccination.\n\nAlthough the government has set the goal of 'eliminating rubella in 20 years,' the disease can affect the Tokyo Olympic Games as well as preventing the attainment of the goal as it is. On 22 Oct 2018, the US Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) recommended that pregnant women who had no vaccination or past history of infection not travel to Japan because rubella was prevalent there.\n\nThe country has a responsibility to take specific measures to stop the outbreak of rubella, but the epidemic continues to expand, and it is also necessary for individuals to take action to prevent infection. People who live in endemic areas and who lack rubella antibodies should undergo antibody testing and vaccination.\n\nWhen a woman up to about 20 weeks gestation is infected with rubella virus, a baby born may develop a disease called 'congenital rubella syndrome,' with heart disease, deafness or cataracts. Because pregnant women cannot receive rubella vaccines, women who do not have antibodies need to be vaccinated twice before pregnancy. Because some people cannot be vaccinated due to immunodeficiency etc., it is necessary to prevent rubella outbreaks in society as a whole.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail Rapporteur Kunihiko Iizuka\n\n[Rubella cases in Japan closely monitored (in Hong Kong): The Centre for Health Protection (CHP) of the Department of Health (DH) [in Hong Kong] is closely monitoring an increase in the number of rubella (also known as 'German measles') cases in Japan and appealed to the travellers and members of the public for vigilance and reiterated that vaccination is the most effective way to prevent the disease.\n\nAccording to the information from the health authority of Japan, an ongoing outbreak of rubella is being observed in the country. A total of 1289 cases of rubella have been recorded in 2018 (as of 17 Oct 2018) with 914 cases reported in the past 6 weeks, as compared to 93, 126 and 163 cases recorded in 2017, 2016 and 2015, respectively. Among these 1289 cases in 2018, 1062 (82.4%) were affected males with 63% of them aged between 30-49 years. The cumulative incidence in 2018 was highest in Chiba (37.6 cases per million population), followed by Tokyo (32 cases per million population) and Kanagawa (17.9 cases per million population).\n\nImmunisation against rubella is the most effective way to prevent the disease. People who intend to travel to Japan are advised to review their vaccination history and past medical history. People who had received rubella-containing vaccine documented by vaccination record, past history of laboratory confirmed rubella infection, or positive blood test for rubella antibody are considered to be immune against rubella. Otherwise, they can be considered as non-immune to rubella.\n\n'As rubella-containing vaccine is contraindicated in pregnant women, all pregnant women and women preparing for pregnancy who are non-immune to rubella should not travel to Japan during the outbreak period,' a spokesman for the CHP said on 23 Oct 2018.\n\nFor those without rubella vaccination, with unknown vaccination history or unknown immunity against rubella, they are advised to consult their doctor for advice on vaccination, which is usually given together with mumps and rubella vaccines as the measles, mumps and rubella (MMR) combined vaccine. As it takes about 2 weeks after vaccination for antibodies to develop and provide protection against rubella, non-immune travellers are advised to plan and get vaccinated ahead, except pregnant women and women preparing for pregnancy (Excerpted from https://www.info.gov.hk/gia/general/201810/23/P2018102300850.htm).\n\nJapan has not had a consistent vaccination policy against measles, mumps, rubella, and as a result, there are pockets of non-immune, susceptible individuals in the country. See the following publication:\n\nY Tanaka et al. History repeats itself in Japan: Failure to learn from rubella epidemic leads to failure to provide the HPV vaccine. Hum Vaccin Immunother. 2017 Jul; 13(8): 1859-1860. doi: [10.1080/ 21645515.2017.1327929].\n\nDue to gaps in herd immunization resulting from politicized transitions in vaccination policy by the [Japanese] government, there were outbreaks of rubella with 17 050 cases reported between the years of 2012 and 2014 and 45 cases of congenital rubella syndrome reported to the National Epidemiological Surveillance of Infectious Diseases from week 1, 2012 to week 40, 2014. Currently, the majority of recent rubella epidemic cases are occurring among adults aged 20-40. By: Ian NjeruDickens OnyangoYusuf AjackElizabeth Kiptoo. BMC Infectious Diseases 2015, 15:245.\n---------------------\nIntroduction\n----------\nRubella infection has been identified as a leading cause of birth defects commonly known as congenital rubella syndrome (CRS). Kenya does not currently have a rubella immunization program or a CRS surveillance system. In 2014, a rubella outbreak was reported in a rural district in Kenya. We investigated the outbreak to determine its magnitude and describe the outbreak in time, place and person.\n\nMethods\n--------\nWe analyzed the laboratory-confirmed rubella cases from 2010 to 2014 to understand the burden of the disease in the country. The Rubella outbreak was detected using the case-based measles surveillance system. A suspected case was a person with generalized rash and fever, while a confirmed case was a person who tested positive for rubella IgM.\n\nAll laboratory-confirmed and epidemiologically linked cases were line listed. The measles case-based surveillance database was used to identify rubella cases from 2010 to 2014.\n\nResults\n------\nA total of 125 rubella cases were line listed. 54 percent of cases were female. Case age ranged from 3 months to 32 years with a median of 4 years.\n\n51 percent were aged less than 5 years, while 82 percent were aged less than 10 years. Six percent of the cases were women of reproductive age.\n\nAll cases were treated as outpatients, and there were no deaths. The number of confirmed rubella cases was 473 in 2010, 604 in 2011, 300 in 2012, 336 in 2013 and 646 in 2014.\n\nConclusions:\n----------\nAnalysis of Kenya rubella data shows that rubella is endemic throughout the country, and many outbreaks may be underestimated or undocumented. Six percent of all the cases in this outbreak were women of reproductive age, indicating that the threat of CRS is real. The country should consider initiating a CRS surveillance system to quantify the burden with the goal of introducing rubella vaccine in the future.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[This retrospective analysis of a rubella outbreak in a rural Kenyan District in 2014 clearly demonstrates the need for routine rubella immunization in rural Kenya - Mod.CP\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/174.]", "summary": "Communicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[This retrospective analysis of a rubella outbreak in a rural Kenyan District in 2014 clearly demonstrates the need for routine rubella immunization in rural Kenya - Mod. Since the case had not recently traveled overseas, Taiwan CDC determined the case to be an indigenous case. The possible source of infection is still being investigated.\n\nTo prevent further transmission of the disease, the health authorities has implemented a number of prevention measures and identified 121 contacts, including his family members, colleagues, and healthcare personnel and patients that he came in contact with when he sought medical attention, to monitor and follow up until [18 Feb 2015]. Currently, besides the case's wife who developed cough, none of the other contacts has developed any suspected symptoms. In addition, rubella infection has been eliminated from the case's wife.\n\nIn 2014, Taiwan reported 7 rubella cases, 6 of which were imported. Rubella infection in people is typically mild. However, in pregnant women, rubella can cause death or birth defects such as cataracts, deafness, glaucoma, and heart, lung and brain abnormalities in an unborn baby (fetus) during the 1st few months of pregnancy. Rubella is a highly contagious disease.\n\nPeople who are infected with rubella are infectious from 1 week before to at least 4 days after the rash appears. It is transmitted through airborne droplets or close contact with the patient, but it is vaccine-preventable.\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n['Rubella is an acute, contagious viral infection. While the illness is generally mild in children, it has serious consequences in pregnant women, causing fetal death or congenital defects known as congenital rubella syndrome (CRS). The rubella virus is transmitted by airborne droplets when infected people sneeze or cough. Humans are the only known host. In children, the disease is usually mild, with symptoms including a rash, low fever (under 39 deg C [102.2 deg F]), nausea, and mild conjunctivitis. The rash, which occurs in 50-80 per cent of cases, usually starts on the face and neck before progressing down the body and lasts 1-3 days. Swollen lymph glands behind the ears and in the neck are the most characteristic clinical feature. Infected adults, more commonly women, may develop arthritis and painful joints that usually last from 3-10 days. Once a person is infected, the virus spreads throughout the body in about 5-7 days. Symptoms usually appear 2-3 weeks after exposure. The most infectious period is usually 1-5 days after the appearance of the rash.\n\n'When a woman is infected with the rubella virus early in pregnancy, she has a 90 per cent chance of passing the virus on to her fetus. This can cause miscarriage, stillbirth or severe birth defects known as CRS. Infants with CRS may excrete the virus for a year or more. Children with CRS can suffer hearing impairments, eye, and heart defects and other lifelong disabilities, including autism, diabetes mellitus, and thyroid dysfunction, many of which require costly therapy, surgeries, and other expensive care.\n\n'The rubella vaccine is a live [naturally] attenuated strain that has been in use for more than 40 years. A single dose gives more than 95 per cent long-lasting immunity, which is similar to that induced by natural infection.' (http://www.who.int/mediacentre/factsheets/fs367/en/).\n\n[The] rubella vaccine is administered as part of the triple MMR [measles, mumps, and rubella] vaccine. The occurrence of rubella disease is an indication of less than adequate uptake of MMR vaccine.\n\nFurther information on this case and a statement regarding current vaccination policy in Taiwan would be welcomed. - Mod.CP\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/193.]", "summary": "It is transmitted through airborne droplets or close contact with the patient, but it is vaccine-preventable.\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n['Rubella is an acute, contagious viral infection. This achievement culminates a 15-year effort that involved widespread administration of the vaccine against measles, mumps and rubella (MMR) throughout the Western Hemisphere. The announcement comes as 45 countries and territories of the Americas are participating in the 13th annual Vaccination Week in the Americas (25 Apr to 2 May 2015).\n\nThe declaration of elimination, made by an international expert committee during a meeting at the Pan American Health Organization/World Health Organization (PAHO/WHO) last week, makes rubella and congenital rubella syndrome (CRS) the 3rd and 4th vaccine-preventable diseases to be eliminated from the Americas, following the regional eradication of smallpox in 1971 and the elimination of polio in 1994. 'The elimination of rubella from the Americas is a historic achievement that reflects the collective will of our region's countries to work together to achieve ambitious public health milestones,' said PAHO/WHO Director Carissa F. Etienne. 'Ours was the 1st region to eradicate smallpox, the 1st to eliminate polio, and now the 1st to eliminate rubella. All 4 achievements prove the value of immunization and how important it is to make vaccines available even to the remotest corners of our hemisphere.'\n\n'Three years ago, governments agreed [on] a Global Vaccine Action Plan. One of the plan's targets is to eliminate rubella from 2 WHO regions by end-2015. I congratulate the Americas Region for being the 1st region to achieve this,' said Dr Margaret Chan, director general of the World Health Organization.\n\nRubella, also known as German measles, caused widespread outbreaks throughout the Americas before the introduction of the MMR vaccine. Although the virus usually causes mild or asymptomatic infections in children and adults, when contracted by women early in pregnancy, it can cause miscarriage or CRS, a constellation of birth defects that often includes blindness, deafness, and congenital heart defects. Before mass-scale rubella vaccination, an estimated 16 000 to more than 20 000 children were born with CRS each year in Latin America and the Caribbean, while more than 158 000 rubella cases were reported in 1997 alone. In the United States, 20 000 infants were born with CRS during the last major rubella outbreak (1964-65).\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[According to CIDRAP (Center for Infectious Disease Research and Policy) News: 'Carissa Etienne, MD, PAHO director, said in the release, 'The fight against rubella has taken more than 15 years, but it has paid off with what I believe will be one of the most important Pan American public health achievements of the 21st century,' she said. PAHO estimated that before mass rubella immunization, about 16 000 to 20 000 children in the region were born with CRS-related birth defects each year.\n\n''Now it's time to roll up our sleeves and finish the job of eliminating measles, as well,' Etienne added.\n\n'Rubella and CRS are the 3rd and 4th vaccine-preventable diseases to be eliminated from the Americas. Concentrated public health efforts eliminated smallpox in 1971 and polio in 1994.\n\n'Margaret Chan, MD, MPH, director-general of the World Health Organization (WHO), said in the statement that 3 years ago, countries agreed to a Global Vaccine Action Plan, which set a goal of eliminating rubella from 2 WHO regions by the end of 2015. Taiwan: ex Philippines", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=5091450", "date": "2017-06-08 11:12:45", "main_text": "RUBELLA - TAIWAN: ex PHILIPPINES\n********************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Wed 7 Jun 2017 4:58 pm TWN\nSource: The China Post [edited]\nhttp://www.chinapost.com.tw/taiwan/national/national-news/2017/06/07/498417/get-vaccinated!.htm\n\n\nHealth authorities have confirmed this year's [2017] 1st case of German measles [rubella] in Taiwan.\n\nAccording to the Centers for Disease Control (CDC), a 34-year-old man from Northern Taiwan complained of headaches, coughing, and rashes after returning from a language course from the Philippines on [4 Jun 2017].\n\nDoctors later diagnosed the man with German measles.\n\nAuthorities are now contacting over 60 people that the man may have come into contact with, including friends, family members, and passengers aboard the Eva Air flight (BR-272) he took to return to Taiwan.\n\nCurrently none of those contacted have developed symptoms of the virus.\n\nCDC officials say that the man traveled to the Philippines on 11 Mar [2017] and started to experience coughing on 27 May [2017], while experiencing headaches and rashes 2 days later. Local doctors diagnosed the man with measles.\n\nThe man boarded the flight bound for Taiwan believing he was no longer ill, but chose to wear a surgical mask as a precaution.\n\nHealth authorities say that because the [German] measles' infection period (22 May-5 Jun 2017) had almost run its course and that the man had worn a surgical mask, the possibility of further infection in Taiwan was remote.\n\nThe CDC will continue monitoring the man's health conditions until [25 Jun 2017].\n\nThe CDC said that over 95 percent of Taiwanese citizens are immune to German measles through previous infection or vaccinations, and that most physical reactions to the virus are mild.\n\nBut the measles are a threat to unborn babies when they are contracted by the mother.\n\nPossible side effects in fetuses include deafness, glaucoma, cataracts, other congenital defects, stillbirths, and spontaneous abortion.\n\nAuthorities issued a reminder that women of childbearing age should be vaccinated against the [German] measles. Local clinics offer vaccines free of charge for German measles, measles and mumps.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Rubella (German measles) is a contagious disease caused by a virus [family Togaviridae, genus _Rubivirus_]. Most people who get rubella usually have a mild illness, with symptoms that can include a low-grade fever, sore throat, and a rash that starts on the face and spreads to the rest of the body. Rubella can cause a miscarriage or serious birth defects in an developing baby if a woman is infected while she is pregnant. The best protection against rubella is MMR (measles-mumps-rubella) vaccine.\n\nThe virus continues to circulate widely, especially in Africa, the Eastern Mediterranean, and Southeast Asian regions. The infection is usually mild with fever and rash. The average incubation period is 14 days (range, 12-23 days).\n\nRubella usually causes the following symptoms in children: a rash that starts on the face and spreads to the rest of the body and low fever (less than 101 F [38 C]). These symptoms last 2 or 3 days. Older children and adults may also have swollen glands and symptoms like a cold before the rash appears. Aching joints occur in many cases, especially among young women. About half of the people who get rubella do not have symptoms.\n\nComplications include birth defects if infection is acquired by a pregnant woman: deafness, cataracts, heart defects, mental retardation, and liver and spleen damage (at least a 20 percent chance of damage to the fetus if a woman is infected early in pregnancy).\n\nThe virus is spread person to person through coughing and sneezing through contact or droplets shed from the respiratory secretions of infected people. People may shed virus from 7 days before the onset of the rash to approximately 5-7 days after rash onset. Transmission from mother to fetus can also occur, with the highest risk of congenital rubella syndrome (CRS) if infection occurs in the 1st trimester. Infants with CRS can transmit virus for up to one year after birth.\n\nRubella vaccine (contained in MMR vaccine) can prevent this disease. Even though endemic rubella virus transmission has been interrupted in the Americas, rubella virus continues to circulate widely, especially in Africa, the Eastern Mediterranean, and Southeast Asian regions. In 2013, 2 large outbreaks were reported in Poland and Japan; cases were mostly among adolescent boys and adult men, but pregnant women were also affected, and their children subsequently developed CRS. In the United States, endemic rubella virus transmission has been eliminated since 2001, but cases continue to be imported. From 2004 to 2013, a median of 10 (range, 4-18) imported cases were reported annually in the United States, and 6 CRS cases were reported during the same period (excerpted from http://www.cdc.gov/rubella/about/index.html).\n\nGongshan Derung and Nu Autonomous County are in Yunnan province in the People's Republic of China. The Institute reported last month [May 2013] that 5000 cases were recorded in the 1st 4 months of 2013. That implies that the past 2 months have easily doubled the number of cases.\n\nThe latest week's cases were highest in Osaka and Tokyo Prefectures with 129 and 82 new cases, respectively. Mapping the spread of the virus may no longer be based on prefecture proximity, as both prefectures are separated by the Chubu Region, or central Japan. With about 800 cases every 7 days during the earlier weeks, the National Institute of Infectious Diseases should have reckoned its effect with such escalating cases. The epidemic has been more prevalent among men from 20 to 40 years old, amounting to 75 percent of the cases.\n\nAlso known as German measles or 3-day measles, rubella is considered a mild infection except for those who are pregnant. The virus, when caught by someone pregnant, may cause severe congenital complications like growth and mental retardation, deafness, and heart defects among others. Rubella may also cause stillbirths. The symptoms of rubella are not easily noticed and may be mistaken as those of flu, so pregnant women are always told to take precautions especially during the 1st trimester of pregnancy.\n\nA call for the government to fund anti-rubella vaccine has been raised. However, Norihisa Tamura of the Ministry of Health, Labor, and Welfare said that Japan's rubella cases, the 10 000 cases as of June 2013, are 'not overwhelmingly larger' compared to other infectious diseases. Adults, especially those with immunization, may be able to survive the virus but given that the symptoms of rubella are similar to those of other communicable diseases, pregnant women and small children are likely to be at risk. Something as simple as MMR [measles, mumps, and rubella] vaccine will be helpful to Japan's small and unborn children. It may even help preserve the country's future population.\n\n[Byline: Faith Aquino]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail Rapporteur Kunihiko Iizuka\n\n[In 1976, Japan introduced single-antigen rubella vaccine in its national immunization program, targeting girls in junior high school. In 1989, a measles, mumps, rubella (MMR) vaccine was introduced, targeting children aged 12-72 months. However, adult males remain susceptible to rubella. It was reported in the previous ProMED-mail report that between 1 Jan 2013 and 1 May 2013, a total of 5442 rubella cases had been recorded throughout Japan, with the majority (77 percent) of cases occurring among adult males.\n\nAlso a total of 10 infants with congenital rubella syndrome (CRS) were reported from October 2012 to [1 May 2013]. Of the mothers of infants with CRS, 6 had not received rubella vaccine, and 4 had unknown vaccination history.\n\nThere appears to be continued reluctance by the Japanese government to extend rubella vaccination to include those males now between 20 and 40 years of age, who had not previously received rubella vaccination as a consequence of government policy. While rubella infection in these individuals does not have serious consequences for them, vaccination would reduce the prevalence of the virus in the community and help to protect those mothers and children, who for whatever reason, had escaped vaccination earlier.\n\nA map of the prefectures of Japan can be accessed at http://gojapan.about.com/cs/japanmaps/l/blmap.htm. - Mod.CP\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/r/62Cn.]", "summary": "It was reported in the previous ProMED-mail report that between 1 Jan 2013 and 1 May 2013, a total of 5442 rubella cases had been recorded throughout Japan, with the majority (77 percent) of cases occurring among adult males. On the following day [15 Mar 2015], she sought medical attention at a medical center. On [18 Mar 2015], rubella infection was confirmed. Prior to her disease onset, she had traveled to Viet Nam, Indonesia, and Hong Kong. As a result, the Taiwan CDC has determined her to be an imported case and the 2nd confirmed rubella case in Taiwan this year [2015].\n\nAccording to health officials, she worked on 6 different flights, including flights CI116 and CI117 that traveled to and from Taoyuan [municipality in northwestern Taiwan] and Fukuoka [prefecture on the northern shore Kyushu Island, Japan] on [8 Mar 2015], flights CI110 and CI111 that traveled to and from Taoyuan and Fukuoka on 10 and 11 Mar 2015, and flights CI753 and CI754 that traveled to and from Taoyuan and Singapore on 13 and 14 Mar 2015.\n\nContact tracing has identified 686 contacts, including her family members, her fellow crew members, passengers on the same flights, healthcare personnel, and patients that she came into contact with when she sought medical attention, to monitor and follow up until [11 Apr 2015].\n\nPassengers on the flights are urged to contact their local health officials and to be aware of rubella symptoms and monitoring their health for the next 21 days. Pregnant women who took the same flight are urged to seek medical attention to determine the need for treatment.\n\nAccording to the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC), rubella, sometimes called German measles or 3-day measles, is a contagious disease caused by a virus. The infection is usually mild with fever and rash. It is spread by contact with an infected person through coughing and sneezing.\n\nWhile symptoms of rubella are typically mild, a rash that starts on the face and spreads to the rest of the body with low fever and body aches, if a pregnant woman contracts rubella virus, her baby could have birth defects such as deafness, cataracts, heart defects, mental disabilities, and organ damage. The health problems due to the infection are called congenital rubella syndrome.\n\n[Byline: Robert Herriman]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-MBDS from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed-mbds@promedmail.org>\n\n[Rubella is a contagious, generally mild viral infection that occurs most often in children and young adults. In pregnant women, this disease may cause fetal death or congenital defects known as congenital rubella syndrome (CRS). Worldwide, an estimated 110 000 babies are born with CRS every year. While there is no specific treatment for rubella, the disease is preventable by vaccination.\n\nThe rubella virus is transmitted by airborne droplets when infected people sneeze or cough. Humans are the only known host.\n\nIn children, the disease is usually mild, with symptoms including a rash, low fever (less than 39 C / 102.2 F), nausea, and mild conjunctivitis. The rash, which occurs in 50-80 percent of cases, usually starts on the face and neck before progressing down the body, and lasts 1-3 days. Swollen lymph glands behind the ears and in the neck are the most characteristic clinical feature. Infected adults, more commonly women, may develop arthritis and painful joints that usually last from 3-10 days.\n\nOnce a person is infected, the virus spreads throughout the body in about 5-7 days. Symptoms usually appear 2 to 3 weeks after exposure. The most infectious period is usually 1-5 days after the appearance of the rash.\n\nWhen a woman is infected with the rubella virus early in pregnancy, she has a 90 percent chance of passing the virus on to her fetus. This can cause miscarriage, stillbirth, or severe birth defects known as CRS. Infants with CRS may excrete the virus for a year or more.\n\nChildren with CRS can suffer hearing impairments, eye and heart defects, and other lifelong disabilities, including autism, diabetes mellitus, and thyroid dysfunction, many of which require costly therapy, surgeries, and other expensive care.\n\nThe highest risk of CRS is in countries where women of childbearing age do not have immunity to the disease (either through vaccination or from having had rubella). Before the introduction of the vaccine, up to 4 babies in every 1000 live births were born with CRS.\n\nLarge-scale rubella vaccination during the past decade has practically eliminated rubella and CRS in many developed and in some developing countries. The WHO Region of the Americas has had no endemic (naturally-transmitted) cases of rubella infection since 2009. CRS rates are highest in the WHO African and Southeast Asian regions where vaccine coverage is lowest.\n\nThe rubella vaccine is a live attenuated strain that has been in use for more than 40 years. A single dose gives more than 95 percent long-lasting immunity, which is similar to that induced by natural infection.\n\nRubella vaccines are available either in monovalent formulation (vaccine directed at only one pathogen) or more commonly in combinations with other vaccines such as with vaccines against measles (MR), measles and mumps (MMR), or measles, mumps, and varicella (MMRV).\n\nAdverse reactions following vaccination are generally mild. They may include pain and redness at the injection site, low-grade fever, rash, and muscle aches. Mass immunization campaigns in the Region of the Americas involving more than 250 million adolescents and adults did not identify any serious adverse reactions associated with the vaccine (http://www.who.int/mediacentre/factsheets/fs367/en/).\n\nIt is understood that all the 686 contacts with the index cases are mostly those with no history of either prior natural disease occurrence or rubella immunization (95 percent protection). While pregnant women are at the highest and immediate risk, male contacts will disseminate the disease further to this former particular risk group.\n\nMaps of Taiwan can be seen at http://www.mapsofworld.com/taiwan/taiwan-political-map.htm and http://healthmap.org/promed/p/193. - Mod.ST]", "summary": "\n\nWhile symptoms of rubella are typically mild, a rash that starts on the face and spreads to the rest of the body with low fever and body aches, if a pregnant woman contracts rubella virus, her baby could have birth defects such as deafness, cataracts, heart defects, mental disabilities, and organ damage. A China Airlines female flight attendant who contracted the viral disease rubella, or German measles, had worked on 6 different flights during her period of communicability, according to Taiwanese health authorities. \n\nThe rubella virus is transmitted by airborne droplets when infected people sneeze or cough. In children, the disease is usually mild, with symptoms including a rash, low fever (less than 39 C / 102.2 F), nausea, and mild conjunctivitis. In pregnant women, this disease may cause fetal death or congenital defects known as congenital rubella syndrome (CRS). \n\nChildren with CRS can suffer hearing impairments, eye and heart defects, and other lifelong disabilities, including autism, diabetes mellitus, and thyroid dysfunction, many of which require costly therapy, surgeries, and other expensive care.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "measles", "mumps", "rubella", "varicella"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2009-03-25 00:00:00", "2015-03-25 00:00:00", "2021-03-13 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Hong Kong", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Japan", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Viet Nam", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Singapore", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Indonesia", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Fukuoka", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Taiwan", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Taoyuan", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2982620", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Rubella - Taiwan: 1st indigenous case", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2982620", "date": "2014-11-22 17:51:38", "main_text": "RUBELLA - TAIWAN: 1ST INDIGENOUS CASE\n*************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Fri 21 Nov 2014\nSource: Outbreak News Today [edited]\nhttp://outbreaknewstoday.com/taiwan-reports-1st-indigenous-case-of-german-measles-of-2014/\n\n\nThe Taiwan Centers for Disease Control (Taiwan CDC) has reported this year's [2014] 6th rubella (German measles) case in a 31-year-old male who resides in northern Taiwan. However, unlike the other 5 cases, this one was acquired in Taiwan.\n\nThe young boy displayed the characteristic maculopapular rash indicative of rubella, otherwise known as German measles. According to the case, on [7 Nov 2014], he developed fever, runny nose and rash. On the following day, he sought medical attention. On [11 Nov 2014], rubella infection was confirmed in the case. Currently, the case has been isolated for medical treatment. Since the case had not recently traveled overseas, Taiwan CDC determined the case to be an indigenous case. The possible source of infection is being investigated.\n\nPreventive measures have been put in place to avoid further transmission. More than 200 contacts are being monitored.\n\nBesides the current indigenous case, the Taiwan CDC reports 2 cases who acquired the infection in China, and one each were acquired in the Philippines, Indonesia and Malaysia.\n\nRubella, or German measles, is a disease spread by the coughs and sneezes of infected people. Symptoms include rash and fever for 2 to 3 days. Some people do not feel sick. If a pregnant woman gets rubella virus, her baby could have birth defects such as deafness, cataracts, heart defects, mental disabilities, and organ damage.\n\nPrevention of rubella is through vaccination. In addition to vaccination, they recommend practicing good hygiene (handwashing) and keeping hands out of faces as other measures to protect yourself.\n\n[Byline: Robert Herriman]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[From ProMED-mail moderator CP's comments in ProMED-mail post Rubella - UK: England (Hampshire), alert 20120912.1291874:\n\n'Rubella is an acute, contagious viral infection. While the illness is generally mild in children, it has serious consequences in pregnant women, causing fetal death or congenital defects known as congenital rubella syndrome (CRS). The rubella virus is transmitted by airborne droplets when infected people sneeze or cough. Humans are the only known host. In children, the disease is usually mild, with symptoms including a rash, low fever (under 39 C), nausea and mild conjunctivitis. The rash, which occurs in 50-80 percent of cases, usually starts on the face and neck before progressing down the body and lasts 1-3 days. Swollen lymph glands behind the ears and in the neck are the most characteristic clinical feature. Infected adults, more commonly women, may develop arthritis and painful joints that usually last from 3-10 days. Once a person is infected, the virus spreads throughout the body in about 5-7 days. Symptoms usually appear 2-3 weeks after exposure. The most infectious period is usually 1-5 days after the appearance of the rash.\n\n'When a woman is infected with the rubella virus early in pregnancy, she has a 90 percent chance of passing the virus on to her fetus. This can cause miscarriage, stillbirth or severe birth defects known as CRS. Infants with CRS may excrete the virus for a year or more. Children with CRS can suffer hearing impairments, eye and heart defects and other lifelong disabilities, including autism, diabetes mellitus and thyroid dysfunction, many of which require costly therapy, surgeries and other expensive care.\n\n'The rubella vaccine is a live [naturally] attenuated strain that has been in use for more than 40 years. A single dose gives more than 95 percent long-lasting immunity, which is similar to that induced by natural infection. [The] rubella vaccine is administered as part of the triple MMR [measles, mumps and rubella] vaccine. The occurrence of rubella disease is an indication of less than adequate uptake of MMR vaccine.'\n\nRubella has been eliminated in the United States. Travelers going outside the United States are at risk for rubella. People who cannot show that they were vaccinated as children and who have never had rubella should be vaccinated. The only rubella vaccines available in the United States are the measles-mumps-rubella (MMR) and the measles-mumps-rubella-varicella (MMRV) vaccines. Adolescents and adults who have not had rubella or have not been vaccinated with MMR should get 2 doses, separated by at least 28 days; and infants 6 months through 11 months of age should have 1 dose of MMR vaccine if traveling internationally (http://wwwnc.cdc.gov/travel/diseases/rubella).\n\nMaps of Taiwan can be seen at http://www.tmf.org.tw/index.php/maps-taiwan-overall-english-cd and http://healthmap.org/promed/p/193. - Mod.ML]", "summary": "Communicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[From ProMED-mail moderator CP's comments in ProMED-mail post Rubella - UK: England (Hampshire), alert 20120912.1291874:\n\n'Rubella is an acute, contagious viral infection. The rubella virus is transmitted by airborne droplets when infected people sneeze or cough.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "measles", "mumps", "rubella", "varicella"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2021-03-01 00:00:00", "2014-03-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Philippines", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Malaysia", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Taiwan", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Indonesia", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "the United States", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "England", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "China", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "1771486", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Rubella - Japan (09): national epidemic", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=1771486", "date": "2013-06-13 16:55:55", "main_text": "RUBELLA - JAPAN (09): NATIONAL EPIDEMIC\n***************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Fri 14 Jun 2013\nSource: Morb Mortal Wkly Rep (MMWR). 2013; 62(23): 457-462 [edited]\nhttp://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/mm6223a1.htm?s_cid=mm6223a1_w\n\n\nNationwide rubella epidemic -- Japan, 2013\n------------------------------------------\nRubella usually is a mild, febrile rash illness in children and adults; however, infection early in pregnancy, particularly during the 1st 16 weeks, can result in miscarriage, stillbirth, or an infant born with birth defects (that is, congenital rubella syndrome [CRS]). As of 2013, goals to eliminate rubella have been established in 2 World Health Organization regions (the Region of the Americas by 2010 and the European Region by 2015), and targets for accelerated rubella control and CRS prevention have been established by the Western Pacific Region (WPR). In 1976, Japan introduced single-antigen rubella vaccine in its national immunization program, targeting girls in junior high school. In 1989, a measles-mumps-rubella (MMR) vaccine was introduced, targeting children aged 12-72 months. However, adult males remain susceptible to rubella. From [1 Jan 2013] to [1 May 2013], a total of 5442 rubella cases were reported through the rubella surveillance system in Japan, with the majority (77 per cent) of cases occurring among adult males. A total of 10 infants with CRS were reported from October 2012 to [1 May 2013]. Countries and regions establishing a goal of accelerated control or elimination of rubella should review their previous and current immunization policies and strategies to identify and vaccinate susceptible persons and to ensure high population immunity in all cohorts, both male and female.\n\nFrom 1999 to 2007, rubella surveillance in Japan consisted of aggregate case reporting to the pediatric sentinel surveillance system. Cases were reported from a representative sample of about 3000 pediatric inpatient and outpatient medical facilities. In January 2008, the sentinel surveillance systems were replaced by nationwide case-based surveillance for rubella, and all physicians were required to report any clinically diagnosed or laboratory-confirmed rubella case to local health officials. In April 1999, nationwide, case-based surveillance for CRS had been established.\n\nUntil the early 2000s, rubella was endemic in Japan, with periodic epidemics approximately every 5 years and seasonal increases in the spring and summer. The number of reported rubella cases remained at record low levels until 2010, and in 2011, a few outbreaks were reported in the workplace among adult males. In 2012, the number of rubella cases sharply increased to 2392, with the rise in cases continuing into 2013. From [1 Jan 2013] to [1 May 2013], a total of 5442 rubella cases were reported. Of these cases, 3936 (72.3 per cent) were laboratory confirmed. Geographically, over 60 per cent of rubella cases were reported from the Kanto [region] in the eastern part of Japan, comprised of Tokyo and its surrounding prefectures. In recent weeks, the epidemic has expanded from Kanto to other parts of Japan, including Osaka, Hyogo, Aichi, Fukuoka, and Kagoshima. Of the 5442 cases, males accounted for 4213 cases (77.4 per cent), of which 3878 cases (92.0 per cent) were in persons over 20 years old. Of the 4834 cases in persons over 20 years old, 1727 (36 per cent) were in persons aged 30-39 years and 1535 (32 per cent) in persons aged 20-29 years. Among rubella cases, vaccination history was unknown in a majority of cases (3538 [65 per cent]). For the 1904 reported rubella cases with known vaccination status, 1566 (82 per cent) occurred in persons who had not received rubella vaccine. Virus genotypes were determined for 150 cases in 2012; of these, 123 (82.0 per cent) and 26 (17.0 per cent) were genotypes 2B and 1E, respectively.\n\nFrom 2008 to 2011, 3 cases of CRS were reported nationwide. Since October 2012, 10 CRS cases have been reported from Hyogo (2), Aichi (2), Osaka (2), Tokyo (1), Kagawa (1), Saitama (1), and Kanagawa (1). Of the mothers of infants with CRS, 6 had not received rubella vaccine, and 4 had unknown vaccination history.\n\nPopulation immunity is measured by administrative coverage and seroprevalence surveys. In 2011, administrative measles-rubella (MR) vaccine coverage was 95.3 per cent at age one year, 92.8 per cent at age 5-6 years, 88.1 per cent at age 12-13 years, and 81.4 per cent at age 17-18 years. Population immunity for 8 vaccine-preventable diseases is measured by the National Epidemiological Surveillance of Vaccine Preventable Diseases, an annual, national seroepidemiologic survey conducted among a representative sample of the Japanese population. In 2012, 14 prefectures in Japan joined this serologic survey by measuring rubella hemagglutination inhibition antibody levels in 5094 healthy persons. Among adults aged 30-50 years, seropositivity for rubella antibody (1:8) was 73-86 per cent among males and 97-98 per cent among females.\n\nIn response to the current outbreak, Japan's Ministry of Health, Labor, and Welfare provided guidance to health care authorities. The guidance is to provide information on rubella disease and CRS for pregnant women and their households and encouraged vaccination of the family members of pregnant women (because rubella vaccine is contraindicated in pregnant women) and vaccination for women who plan to get pregnant. The local governments in approximately 100 cities, including several districts in the Tokyo metropolitan area that had high numbers of reported rubella cases, have provided partial funding to help with the cost of MR vaccine or a single rubella vaccine for women planning pregnancy and for men who are living with a pregnant woman. In addition, mass media agencies in Japan have provided information about the rubella epidemic, including rubella disease and CRS, which has helped increase awareness about the importance of rubella vaccination.\n\nMMWR editorial note\n-------------------\nThe primary purpose of rubella vaccination is to prevent congenital rubella virus infection, including CRS. In WPR, the Immunization Technical Advisory Group endorsed a regional accelerated rubella control and CRS prevention goal to decrease rubella incidence to less than 10 cases per million population and CRS incidence to less than 10 cases per million live births each year by 2015. In 2012, Japan reported 18.7 rubella cases per million population, a rate higher than the WPR annual incidence target. As of May 2013 (4 months into the year), the number of reported rubella cases is already double the total number of cases in 2012.\n\nIn 1976, Japan established a goal to prevent CRS and introduced single-antigen rubella vaccine in its national immunization program, targeting girls in junior high school. In 1989, an MMR vaccine was introduced, targeting children aged 12-72 months, but this combination vaccine was withdrawn in 1993 after reports of aseptic meningitis related to the mumps component. In 1995, vaccination policy was changed to make all vaccines strongly recommended but not mandatory, and in 2006, the MR combined vaccine was introduced, with a 2-dose schedule administered at 1-2 years and 5-7 years. After a large measles outbreak in 2007 and 2008, a catch-up MR vaccination program was implemented, targeting 2 age cohorts (those aged 12 years and those aged 17 years) each year from 2008 to 2013 to ensure high population immunity among persons aged 12-22 years in 2013.\n\nIn the current outbreak, males aged 20-39 years, who were not included in the initial rubella vaccination program, accounted for 68 per cent of the reported cases. However, with the introduction of 2 doses of MR vaccine into the national vaccination schedule in 2006 for both boys and girls and the successful catch-up vaccination program, children who are currently under 15 years old account for only 5.6 per cent of the cases. In other countries (for example, Brazil, Chile, and Argentina), where only adolescent or adult females have been targeted through national immunization programs or as part of mass vaccination campaigns, similar large outbreaks have occurred among adolescent and adult males, with a concomitant increase in CRS cases. These types of outbreaks emphasize that national immunization programs should ensure high levels of immunity in all cohorts born since the introduction of rubella vaccine (both males and females) either through the routine program or high-quality mass campaigns that are sufficient to interrupt rubella virus transmission and prevent CRS cases. In addition, programs should implement high-quality, case-based rubella and CRS surveillance and respond promptly and rapidly to outbreaks.\n\nThe effects of this outbreak have been wide-ranging, both within Japan and internationally. In the Region of the Americas, where endemic rubella virus transmission has been interrupted, importations have occurred in the United States and Canada in 2013. The international spread of rubella virus from Japan provides a reminder that countries in regions that have eliminated rubella need to maintain high levels of vaccination coverage and high-quality surveillance to limit the spread and detect imported rubella virus.\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Congenital rubella syndrome (CRS) is caused by fetal infection with rubella virus from the mother, and is characterized by birth defects such as hearing impairment, heart defects, and cataracts. Several countries that initially vaccinated only adolescent or adult women, then later introduced rubella vaccine into their routine programs or conducted mass campaigns in adolescent and adult females, have experienced large rubella outbreaks among adolescent and young adult males, with a concomitant increase in infants with CRS. In 2012, the number of rubella cases in Japan sharply increased to 2392, with the rise in cases continuing into 2013 and resulting in a cumulative total of 5442 cases from 1 Jan 2013 to 1 May 2013. Of these cases, 23 per cent were in females. Since October 2012, 10 congenital rubella syndrome cases have been reported. Countries using rubella vaccine should aim to prevent rubella outbreaks by ensuring high rubella immunity across all age groups (both males and females). In cohorts born since the introduction of rubella vaccine, this immunity is achieved primarily through uniformly high vaccination coverage. - Mod.CP\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/r/62Cn.]", "summary": "From [1 Jan 2013] to [1 May 2013], a total of 5442 rubella cases were reported through the rubella surveillance system in Japan, with the majority (77 per cent) of cases occurring among adult males.
