We have 47 prefectures in Japan; the epidemiological situation varies from prefecture to prefecture. 204 cases of rubella were reported from Osaka prefecture that week, the most, followed by Tokyo metropolitan (112 cases), Hyogo prefecture (85 cases), Kanagawa prefecture (61 cases), Wakayama prefecture (33 cases), and Chiba prefecture (24 cases). [Data and figure available at http://www0.nih.go.jp/niid/idsc/idwr/diseases/rubella/rubella2013/rube13-21.pdf (partly in Japanese)].\n\nThe number of reported cases in these 6 prefectures amount to 77 percent of the total in Japan. All of these 6 prefectures have begun to subsidize vaccination fees for adult people who are at risk. The governments of many other prefectures or cities are also implementing subsidisation.\n\n--\nTsuyoshi Ogata, MD, PhD\nDirector\nChikusei Public Health Centre\nIbaraki Prefectural Government\nJapan\n<t.ogata@pref.iba>\n\n[ProMED-mail and this moderator thank Dr Ogata for providing this new information recording the extension of cost-free immunisation to include those members of the adult population who may be at risk as a consequence of previous vaccination policy. - Mod.CP\n\nMaps of Japan can be seen at http://mapsof.net/uploads/static-maps/cities_of_japan_map.png and http://healthmap.org/r/62Cn. - Sr.Tech.Ed.MJ]", "summary": "204 cases of rubella were reported from Osaka prefecture that week, the most, followed by Tokyo metropolitan (112 cases), Hyogo prefecture (85 cases), Kanagawa prefecture (61 cases), Wakayama prefecture (33 cases), and Chiba prefecture (24 cases). Although pregnancy is stated as contraindication, if inadvertently given to a woman with even early pregnancy, the vaccine is safe and no fetal problems should occur (based on earlier reviews and assessment of the WHO Global Advisory Committee on Vaccine Safety). So a pulse campaign is quite safe and feasible. Adults are urged to get vaccinated, as the disease is likely to peak around June, Japan's NHK broadcaster reported on Wednesday [8 May 2013].\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[There seems no end in sight to the current outbreak of rubella virus infection in Japan. RUBELLA - JAPAN (05): STILL SPREADING\n*************************************\n", "reports": [{"diseases": ["measles", "rubella"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2021-03-31 00:00:00", "2013-05-08 00:00:00", "2021-06-25 00:00:00", "2012-04-25 00:00:00", "2021-03-04 00:00:00", "2021-03-05 00:00:00", "2020-03-25 00:00:00", "2000-03-25 00:00:00", "2015-03-25 00:00:00", "5442-03-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Japan", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "1754725", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Rubella - Japan (07): update", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=1754725", "date": "2013-06-04 16:17:00", "main_text": "RUBELLA - JAPAN (07): UPDATE\n****************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Tue 4 Jun 2013\nFrom: Kentaro Iwata <kentaroiwata1969@gmail.com> [edited]\n\n\n[This report is an update and revised version of previous reports by Dr Mugen Ujiie, Tokyo, Japan. - Mod.CP]\n\nA rubella epidemic still continues in Japan. The epidemic was mainly in Tokyo, the capital of Japan, but now has spread to almost all over Japan. According to the National Institute of Infectious Diseases, as of week 21, 2013 [week ending 25 May 2013], Osaka area, on the west side of Japan, exceeded Tokyo in regards to the number of rubella cases. 204 cases of either clinically or laboratory-confirmed rubella cases were reported from Osaka that week, followed by Tokyo (112 cases), Hyogo (85 cases), and Kanagawa (61 cases). 672 cases of rubella were reported on week 21 from all over Japan. As of this week, 8507 cases of rubella were reported in total, which is far more than the total number of rubella cases reported in 2012 (2392). The majority of the patients are males in their 30's, who often missed rubella vaccination (vide infra).\n\nAs of [10 May 2013], 5 new cases of congenital rubella syndrome (CRS) have been reported in 2013. In the last decade, 25 cases of CRS were reported in Japan, including those reported this year [2013]. Some municipalities decided to subsidize vaccination fees for those who are at risk. However, Japan's Government has not moved forward to change the current status; it will not subsidize vaccination fees for anybody beyond children on the routine vaccination schedule, nor develop catch-up vaccination program for adolescents or adults at risk.\n\nGenerally, rubella is common in the spring to summer. Therefore, the number of patients may continue to rise. Urgent vaccinations against rubella need to be promoted for all of people who have never had rubella before and have not been immunized against it.\n\nThe original information sources for this report include:\n- National Institute of Infectious disease\nhttp://www0.nih.go.jp/niid/idsc/idwr/diseases/rubella/rubella2013/rube13-21.pdf (partly in Japanese),\nhttp://www.nih.go.jp/niid/en/iasren/2930-inx393-e.html\n- Tokyo Metropolitan Infectious Disease Surveillance Center\nhttp://idsc.tokyo-eiken.go.jp/assets/weekly/2013/10e.pdf\n\nRubella is an acute, viral infection spread via droplets. While the illness is generally mild in children, it has serious consequences in pregnant women, causing fetal death or congenital defects known as congenital rubella syndrome (CRS). When an adult woman is infected with rubella virus early in pregnancy, she has a 90 percent chance of passing the virus on to her fetus. This can cause miscarriage, stillbirth, or severe birth defects known as CRS. Infants with CRS may excrete the virus for a year or more. Children with CRS can suffer hearing impairments, eye and heart defects, and other lifelong disabilities, including autism, diabetes mellitus, and thyroid dysfunction, many of which require costly therapy, surgeries, and other expensive care.\n\nRubella vaccine is normally included as a component of the MMR (measles, mumps, rubella) triple vaccine administered in early childhood. The rubella component of the MMR triple childhood vaccine is a live [naturally] attenuated strain that has been in use for more than 40 years. A single dose gives more than 95 percent long-lasting immunity, which is similar to that induced by natural infection. Because of the occurrence of aseptic meningitis after injection of MMR in 1980's to early 1990's, which was believed to be caused by the mumps component in MMR, the coverage rate of rubella immunization decreased significantly in Japan, particularly in the male population. The Ministry of Health (back then) was intimidated by scores of lawsuits, and was not willing to encourage vaccination programs since then. Japan still has occasional outbreaks of measles and sporadic cases of bacterial meningitis or Japanese encephalitis. These are believed to be partly due to defective vaccination policy and programs in Japan.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nKentaro Iwata, MD, MSc, FACP, FIDSA\nDivision of Infectious Diseases Therapeutics\nKobe University Graduate School of Medicine\nKusunoki-cho 7-5-2, Chuo-ku, Kobe, Hyogo 650-0017\nJapan\n\n[ProMED-mail thanks Kentaro Iwata for provision of this update. It is surprising that under the prevailing circumstances the Japanese government has decided against financing catch-up vaccination. - Mod.CP\n\nMaps of Japan can be seen at http://mapsof.net/uploads/static-maps/cities_of_japan_map.png and http://healthmap.org/r/62Cn. - Sr.Tech.Ed.MJ]", "summary": "\n\nThe original information sources for this report include:\n- National Institute of Infectious disease\nhttp://www0.nih.go.jp/niid/idsc/idwr/diseases/rubella/rubella2013/rube13-21.pdf (partly in Japanese),\nhttp://www.nih.go.jp/niid/en/iasren/2930-inx393-e.html\n- Tokyo Metropolitan Infectious Disease Surveillance Center\nhttp://idsc.tokyo-eiken.go.jp/assets/weekly/2013/10e.pdf\n\nRubella is an acute, viral infection spread via droplets. 204 cases of either clinically or laboratory-confirmed rubella cases were reported from Osaka that week, followed by Tokyo (112 cases), Hyogo (85 cases), and Kanagawa (61 cases). As of 21 Mar 22013, the National Institute of Infectious Diseases in Japan reported a total of 2021 cases of rubella. The highest numbers of cases reported are in Tokyo followed by Kanagawa then Chiba.\n\nRubella is a moderately contagious disease of the respiratory tract caused by rubella virus. The symptoms of rubella include mild fever, rash, runny nose, sore throat, swollen lymph nodes, and aching joints. Although the symptoms of rubella are mild, infection is more severe in early pregnancy and may cause a variety of birth defects or may even lead to a miscarriage.\n\nIt would be advisable for persons traveling to Japan to make sure they have been vaccinated for rubella (given in combination with the measles and mumps vaccines) 2 weeks before departing.\n\nThe DPHSS encourages pregnant women to protect themselves and parents to protect their infants and young children by minimizing exposure (close contact) to persons who have a rash-like illness and cold symptoms. Parents should check their children's immunization (shot) records to ensure they received all age-appropriate vaccines.\n\nThe current recommendations from the US Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) are: children should receive 2 doses of measles, mumps, and rubella (MMR) vaccine, with the 1st dose given at 1 year of age or older and a 2nd dose given between 4-6 years of age. All persons born during or after 1957 should have documentation of at least one dose of MMR vaccine given on or after the 1st birthday.\n\nFurthermore, all health care providers are urged to review and update the immunization status of all patients they see. If vaccination is contraindicated because of illness, a follow-up appointment should be scheduled to update vaccination as soon as the illness is over.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[The reason for the increased incidence of rubella virus infection in Japan is a consequence of past immunisation policy whereby only female members of the population were administered rubella vaccine. RUBELLA - JAPAN (04): TRAVEL ALERT\n**********************************\n \n\nThe DPHSS encourages pregnant women to protect themselves and parents to protect their infants and young children by minimizing exposure (close contact) to persons who have a rash-like illness and cold symptoms. \n\nRubella is a moderately contagious disease of the respiratory tract caused by rubella virus. The rationale for this policy and an account of current practise can be found in the preceding text in this thread: 'Rubella - Japan (03): vaccination policy 20130321.1597149'.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["measles", "mumps", "rubella"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["1957-03-25 00:00:00", "2021-03-04 00:00:00", "2021-03-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Guam", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Japan", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Tokyo", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "1733914", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Rubella - Poland: young male adults, CRS risk", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=1733914", "date": "2013-05-23 23:48:08", "main_text": "RUBELLA - POLAND: YOUNG MALE ADULTS, CRS RISK\n*********************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Thu 23 May 2013\nSource: Euro Surveill. 2013;18(21):pii=20485 [abridged, edited]\nhttp://www.eurosurveillance.org/ViewArticle.aspx?ArticleId=20485\n\n\nOngoing outbreak of rubella among young male adults in Poland: increased risk of congenital rubella infections. (By Paradowska-Stankiewicz I, Czarkowski MP, Derrough T, Stefanoff P.)\n\nBackground\n----------\nFrom January to April 2013, Poland reported 21 283 rubella cases (55.2 per 100 000 inhabitants), the highest number since 2007. Some 81 percent of cases were among 15-29 year-old males. This outbreak reflects the history of immunisation policies, selective vaccination of adolescent girls since 1989, then universal 2-dose measles-mumps-rubella vaccination since 2004. The extent of virus circulation among adults increases the risk of congenital rubella infections and jeopardises the World Health Organization Regional Office for Europe's 2015 elimination goal.\n\nOngoing rubella outbreak\n------------------------\nFrom January to April 2013, physicians in Poland notified 21 283 rubella cases (55.2 per 100 000 inhabitants) [1], a near 10-fold increase compared with the 2224 cases (5.8 per 100 000 inhabitants) reported during the corresponding period of 2012.\n\nPhysicians use the European Union (EU) case definitions for rubella and congenital rubella syndrome (CRS) [2]. They report each rubella case to the local health department, providing demographic information, International Classification of Diseases (ICD)-10 code [3], case classification and vaccination status. Every 2 weeks, local health departments report the number of cases notified in their area to the provincial health departments that aggregate data, which they forward to the National Institute of Public Health - National Institute of Hygiene. Every month, local health departments prepare aggregated reports with more detailed data, including the number of cases by age group, sex, vaccination status and case classification.\n\nPhysicians report CRS cases to the local health departments. Local health departments investigate CRS cases and send individual reports to the National Institute of Public Health - National Institute of Hygiene. There is no routine active case finding search for CRS cases, nor has active CRS surveillance been implemented to date.\n\nWe describe here this country-wide rubella outbreak by person, place and time and propose implementation of preventive measures.\n\nWe calculated reported rates per 100 000 inhabitants, dividing the number of rubella reported cases by mid-year census estimates [4]. The reported rates of rubella varied substantially between provinces (median: 51.2 per 100 000 inhabitants; range: 7.4-151.1 per 100 000 inhabitants). The majority of cases were reported from 3 provinces in the southeast of the country (n=8.659; 41 percent), bordering Ukraine, Belarus and Slovakia, and 5 provinces in the northwest (n=7.997; 38 percent), 2 of which border Germany.\n\nThe male to female ratio was 10:1. The most affected groups were persons who were male aged 15-19 years (12 220 cases, reported rate: 1044.9 per 100 000 inhabitants; 57 percent of cases), 20-24 years (4000 cases, reported rate: 286.8 per 100000 inhabitants; 19 percent of cases) and 25-29 years (992 cases, reported rate: 61.1 per 100 000 inhabitants; 5 percent of cases). During 2003 to 2012 and the 1st 4 months of 2013, rubella reported rates suggested an increasing trend in 2006-2007 and then in 2012, and the 1st 1/3rd of 2013. The increases in 2006-2007 and from 2012 were both more pronounced among males 10 years of age and older, especially in the 1st 4 months of 2013.\n\nVaccination status was recorded for 15 237 (72 percent) reported cases. Of these, 1502 (10 percent) were vaccinated with one dose of rubella-containing vaccine, and 234 (2 percent) with 2 or more doses of rubella-containing vaccine. Of all reported cases, 29 (0.1 percent) were confirmed (based on a valid laboratory test); 57 (0.3 percent) were probable (based on an epidemiological link to a confirmed case), and 21 197 (99.6 percent) were possible (based on clinical symptoms).\n\nFrom January to April 2013, 2 cases of (CRS) were reported, as compared with 4 cases reported during 2003 to 2012.\n\nHistory of rubella immunisation in Poland\n-----------------------------------\nIn 1989, Poland started to administer monovalent rubella vaccine to 13-year-old girls. In 1992-2006, reported coverage ranged from 94 percent to 99 percent [5]. In 2004, measles-mumps-rubella (MMR) vaccine was administered to all children at the age of 13-15 months and 10 years [1]. In 2005-2012, 1st-dose coverage among 3-year-old children ranged from 91 percent to 98 percent [5].\n\nIn 2011, a mission of the World Health Organization (WHO) Regional Office for Europe reviewed the rubella situation in Poland, identified an immunity gap among adolescent males and young adults, and recommended supplementary immunisation of all adolescents and young adults, as the increased circulation of rubella among young adults increases the risk of congenital rubella syndrome (CRS) [6].\n\nDiscussion\n---------\nThe 2013 outbreak of rubella in Poland reinforces the need for public health efforts to meet the WHO Regional Office for Europe target for the elimination of measles and rubella and prevention of CRS by 2015 [7]. From April 2012 to March 2013, rubella cases in Poland comprised 74 percent of those in the EU/European Economic Area (EEA) countries [8]. Despite the visible impact of the childhood immunisation programme in decreasing rubella activity among the vaccinated age groups, Poland will not achieve rubella elimination without targeted supplementary immunisation activities.\n\nThe outbreak reflects the historical immunisation activities in Poland: a combination of selective and universal vaccination led to a shift in the age of rubella infections to cohorts of young adults. This situation increases the risk for CRS, due to high circulation of the rubella virus among adolescents and young adult populations. Despite long-term vaccination of adolescent girls, about 10 percent of women of childbearing age may still be susceptible to rubella, as documented in a 2004 study [9]. From January to April 2013, 2 cases of CRS have already been reported, as compared with 4 cases during 2003 to 2012. Further cases are, unfortunately, expected and unavoidable, as observed during outbreaks in other EU/EEA countries in recent years.\n\nThe country most recently affected was Romania. In 2011-2012, Romania reported 20 772 rubella cases, 22 confirmed CRS cases, and 11 cases of congenital rubella infection [10,11]. A similar situation was observed in Greece in the 1990s: a large rubella outbreak was described in 1993, with 25 cases of CRS, and another epidemic occurred in 1999, mainly in young adults, with 4 cases of CRS [12].\n\nThese observations in Greece, Romania and now Poland are a consequence of immunisation practices that had been followed, leading to the build-up of susceptible cohorts. Sadly, rubella outbreaks in young adults unavoidably lead to children being born with CRS. This situation is likely to be repeated if women of childbearing age are left unprotected and become infected with rubella virus. All possible efforts should be undertaken to prevent any cases of CRS in Europe in the future. The availability of rubella-containing vaccines with a long-standing history of good safety and effectiveness profiles, and provision to all those who need it, would ensure that the potentially dramatic consequences of rubella infection in pregnancy would become a thing of the past.\n\nOur report has one main limitation. The vast majority of cases were reported based on clinical symptoms and were not laboratory confirmed. Thus, the clinical cases could be potentially due to a concomitant outbreak of another illness causing a rash.\n\nThis outbreak empirically reflects the shift in the age of infections and the accumulation of susceptible cohorts 10 years after starting universal vaccination. The increased age of infected people leads to an increased risk of CRS. This situation requires immediate public health action to prevent further CRS cases.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Interested readers should consult the original text via the source URL to view the figures and references cited.\n\nThe eradication of rubella virus infection and elimination of the congenital syndrome are closely linked to vaccination policy as described above. Before the introduction of the MMR vaccine, some countries relied on comprehensive vaccination of female children with rubella vaccine, which achieved a reduction in the frequency of the congenital rubella syndrome (CRS) but could never achieve complete elimination. Recently in ProMED-mail, the consequences of a similar policy in Japan have been described in detail [see ProMED archives below].\n\nFinal eradication of congenital rubella syndrome (CRS) will require universal adoption of MR vaccination. Rubella is a contagious, generally mild viral infection that occurs most often in children and young adults. Rubella infection in pregnant women may cause fetal death or the congenital defects known as congenital rubella syndrome. According to the WHO, worldwide, an estimated 110 000 babies are born with CRS every year. When a woman is infected with rubella virus early in pregnancy, she has a 90 percent chance of passing the virus on to her fetus. This can cause miscarriage, stillbirth or the severe birth defects known as CRS. Children with CRS can suffer hearing impairments, eye and heart defects and other lifelong disabilities, including diabetes mellitus and thyroid dysfunction. Before the introduction of vaccine, up to 4 babies in every 1000 live births were born with CRS. - Mod.CP\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/r/1JDJ.]", "summary": "The most affected groups were persons who were male aged 15-19 years (12 220 cases, reported rate: 1044.9 per 100 000 inhabitants; 57 percent of cases), 20-24 years (4000 cases, reported rate: 286.8 per 100000 inhabitants; 19 percent of cases) and 25-29 years (992 cases, reported rate: 61.1 per 100 000 inhabitants; 5 percent of cases). The 2013 outbreak of rubella in Poland reinforces the need for public health efforts to meet the WHO Regional Office for Europe target for the elimination of measles and rubella and prevention of CRS by 2015 Despite the visible impact of the childhood immunisation programme in decreasing rubella activity among the vaccinated age groups, Poland will not achieve rubella elimination without targeted supplementary immunisation activities. Rubella infection in pregnant women may cause fetal death or the congenital defects known as congenital rubella syndrome. The reported rates of rubella varied substantially between provinces (median: 51.2 per 100 000 inhabitants; range: 7.4-151.1 per 100 000 inhabitants). From January to April 2013, 2 cases of CRS have already been reported, as compared with 4 cases during 2003 to 2012. The majority of cases were reported from 3 provinces in the southeast of the country (n=8.659; 41 percent), bordering Ukraine, Belarus and Slovakia, and 5 provinces in the northwest (n=7.997; 38 percent), 2 of which border Germany. From January to April 2013, physicians in Poland notified 21 283 rubella cases (55.2 per 100 000 inhabitants) From April 2012 to March 2013, rubella cases in Poland comprised 74 percent of those in the EU/European Economic Area (EEA) countries [8]. The availability of rubella-containing vaccines with a long-standing history of good safety and effectiveness profiles, and provision to all those who need it, would ensure that the potentially dramatic consequences of rubella infection in pregnancy would become a thing of the past. This outbreak reflects the history of immunisation policies, selective vaccination of adolescent girls since 1989, then universal 2-dose measles-mumps-rubella vaccination since 2004. \n\nVaccination status was recorded for 15 237 (72 percent) reported cases. A ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n Despite long-term vaccination of adolescent girls, about 10 percent of women of childbearing age may still be susceptible to rubella, as documented in a 2004 study [9]. [1], a near 10-fold increase compared with the 2224 cases (5.8 per 100 000 inhabitants) reported during the corresponding period of 2012. According to the National Institute of Infectious Diseases, 745 cases of rubella infection have been reported from 1st to 7th week [1 Jan-16 Feb] of 2013 in Japan, almost 20 times higher than a year ago. Of the 745 cases of rubella infection, 591 (79 percent) were male. Among the male patients, 88 percent were aged from 20s through 40s. In the background of this trend, there is the fact that boys had not been vaccinated against rubella by the national immunization program until 1995.\n\nLast year's [2012] rubella epidemic hit 2353 people in total, but just 19 in January. Although there were 5 cases of congenital rubella syndrome (CRS) last year, another case of CRS has already been reported in January 2013.\n\nOn 29 Jan 2013, the Ministry of Health, Labor and Welfare issued a nationwide alert about the potential for another epidemic and recommended that prefectures and other local governments start promoting vaccination. However, 133 cases of rubella infection were reported in the 8th week [17-23 Feb 2013] in Tokyo, according to the latest report from Infectious Disease Surveillance Center of Tokyo. The appropriate [preventive measures] including urgent vaccinations are recommended especially for women trying to get pregnant and their husbands.\n\nThe original Information sources for this report include:\n- Seroprevalence of rubella in japan\nhttp://idsc.nih.go.jp/yosoku/Rubella/Serum-R2009-e.html\n- The Japan Times\nhttp://www.japantimes.co.jp/news/2013/02/08/national/rubella-outbreak-spreading-quickly/#.US-lQqXvOHs\n- Tokyo City Information\nhttp://psa.interfm.jp/Tokyomet/?p=409\n- National Institute of Infectious disease\n<http://www.nih.go.jp/niid/images/idsc/disease/rubella/2013pdf/rube13-07.pdf (partly in Japanese)\n- Tokyo eiken\nhttp://survey.tokyo-eiken.go.jp/epidinfo/weeklyzensu.do (in Japanese)\n\n--\nMugen Ujiie, MD, MTM\nNational Center for Global Health and Medicine\nDisease Control and Prevention Center\n1-21-1 Toyama, Shinjuku,\nTokyo, 162-8655,\nJapan\n<infinite_u@hotmail.com>\n\n[As a result of the circumstance that in Japan prior to 1995 male children were not administered rubella vaccine, there is currently a marked increase in rubella virus infection among males aged predominantly between 20 and 40 years of age.\n\nRubella is an acute, contagious viral infection. While the illness is generally mild in children, it has serious consequences in pregnant women, causing fetal death or congenital defects known as congenital rubella syndrome (CRS). Rubella virus is transmitted by airborne droplets when infected people sneeze or cough. Humans are the only known host. In children, the disease is usually mild, with symptoms including a rash, low fever (under 39 deg C/102.2 deg F), nausea and mild conjunctivitis. The rash, which occurs in 50-80 percent of cases, usually starts on the face and neck before progressing down the body and lasts 1-3 days. Swollen lymph glands behind the ears and in the neck are the most characteristic clinical feature. Infected adults, more commonly women, may develop arthritis and painful joints that usually last from 3-10 days.\n\nWhen an adult woman is infected with rubella virus early in pregnancy, she has a 90 percent chance of passing the virus on to her fetus. This can cause miscarriage, stillbirth, or severe birth defects known as CRS. Infants with CRS may excrete the virus for a year or more. Children with CRS can suffer hearing impairments, eye and heart defects, and other lifelong disabilities, including autism, diabetes mellitus, and thyroid dysfunction, many of which require costly therapy, surgeries, and other expensive care.\n\nRubella vaccine is normally included as a component of the MMR (measles, mumps, rubella) triple vaccine administered in early childhood. The rubella component of the MMR triple childhood vaccine is a live [naturally] attenuated strain that has been in use for more than 40 years. A single dose gives more than 95 percent long-lasting immunity, which is similar to that induced by natural infection. The reason for the past policy in Japan to withhold rubella vaccine from male children prior to 1995 is unclear.\n\nWhile young males are not badly affected by rubella virus infection, they help to maintain the virus in circulation in the Japanese population. - Mod.CP\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/r/1rMj.]", "summary": "- Tokyo eiken\nhttp://survey.tokyo-eiken.go.jp/epidinfo/weeklyzensu.do (in Japanese)\n\n--\nMugen Ujiie, MD, MTM\nNational Center for Global Health and Medicine\nDisease Control and Prevention Center\n1-21-1 Toyama, Shinjuku,\nTokyo, 162-8655,\nJapan\n<infinite_u@hotmail.com>\n\n A ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n [As a result of the circumstance that in Japan prior to 1995 male children were not administered rubella vaccine, there is currently a marked increase in rubella virus infection among males aged predominantly between 20 and 40 years of age.\n\nRubella is an acute, contagious viral infection. \n\nThe original Information sources for this report include:\n- Seroprevalence of rubella in japan\nhttp://idsc.nih.go.jp/yosoku/Rubella/Serum-R2009-e.html\n- http://psa.interfm.jp/Tokyomet/?p=409\n- National Institute of Infectious disease\n<http://www.nih.go.jp/niid/images/idsc/disease/rubella/2013pdf/rube13-07.pdf (partly in Japanese)\n The Japan Times\nhttp://www.japantimes.co.jp/news/2013/02/08/national/rubella-outbreak-spreading-quickly/#.US-lQqXvOHs\n- Tokyo City Information\n \n\nRubella vaccine is normally included as a component of the MMR (measles, mumps, rubella) triple vaccine administered in early childhood. According to the National Institute of Infectious Diseases, as of week 10, 2013 (as of 13 Mar 2013), 1656 rubella patients have been reported. This number is still rapidly increasing and reaching to 2353 which is the number of rubella cases reported by prefecture and methods of diagnosis in week 52, 2012 (as of 8 Jan 2013).\n\nIn February 2013, a 25-year-old man who had generalized seizure with fever and rash was diagnosed with rubella encephalitis by the detection of rubella virus from throat swab and seroconversion of IgM antibodies at the National Center for Global Health and Medicine hospital in Tokyo. Rubella is known to potentially cause serious complications such as encephalitis. This is the second reported case of encephalitis due to rubella in Japan since last summer.\n\nMoreover, in the 10th week of 2013, a second case of congenital rubella syndrome (CRS) was reported from Aichi prefecture which is located around the middle of Japan. In total 7 cases of CRS has been reported since the beginning of this epidemic of rubella last year [2012].\n\nGenerally, rubella is [more common] in the spring to summer. So the number of patients in the future likely will continue to increase. The Tokyo Prefectural Government on 14 Mar 2013 decided to pay a part of the cost of rubella vaccination in adults aged over 19 years old as emergent preventive measures against this epidemic. Urgent vaccinations against rubella needs to be promoted for all of people who have never had rubella before and have not been immunized against it.\n\nThe original Information sources for this report include:\n- National Institute of Infectious disease\nhttp://www.nih.go.jp/niid/images/idsc/disease/rubella/2013pdf/rube13-10.pdf (partly in Japanese)\nhttp://www.nih.go.jp/niid/en/iasren/2930-inx393-e.html\n- Tokyo Metropolitan Infectious Disease Surveillance Center\nhttp://idsc.tokyo-eiken.go.jp/assets/weekly/2013/10e.pdf\n- Japan Xinhua China Press\nhttp://www.newshome.us/news-4076488-Rubella-pandemic-patients-in-Japan-the-number-of-innovative-high-young-men-accounted-for-60.html\n--\nMugen Ujie, MD, MTM\nNational Center for Global Health and Medicine\nDisease Control and Prevention Center\n1-21-1 Toyama, Shinjuku,\nTokyo, 162-8655, Japan\n\n\n[As a result of the circumstance that in Japan prior to 1995 male children were not administered rubella vaccine. Latest figures have revealed that there have been 14 confirmed cases of the potentially dangerous infection in Hampshire and the Isle of Wight between January and July this year [2012] compared to none during the same period in 2011. Although the infection can easily be prevented by youngsters receiving their measles, mumps and rubella (MMR) jabs, if caught, it can be dangerous, particularly to pregnant women, causing rubella-associated miscarriages and birth defects.\n\nRubella is a viral infection that is spread in a similar way as a cold and will normally pass [end] within 7 to 10 days. But it becomes serious if a pregnant woman catches the infection during the 1st 16 weeks of pregnancy as it can disrupt the development of the baby, causing congenital rubella syndrome which could include cataracts, deafness, heart abnormalities and brain damage.\n\nIt is believed the outbreak has been caused by a dip in the number of children getting vaccinated, forcing health bosses to urge parents into action. To reduce the risk of such outbreaks, health bosses aim to get 95 per cent of 3-year-olds vaccinated, but between January and March this year [2012], only 89 per cent in Hampshire and 91 per cent in Southampton received their pre-school booster MMR jab. Parents are being asked to speak with their health visitors for advice about vaccinations and arrange for their child to get their jabs at their local surgery.\n\nDr Ruth Milton, director of public health in Hampshire, said: 'The uptake for MMR1 should be even better, but we are most concerned about the MMR2, the pre-school booster given after your child is 3. Every child requires 2 doses of the vaccine to protect them from all 3 diseases. When we reach the 95 per cent target, we have herd immunity, where enough of the population is immunised to protect those that aren't. Parents have a really important role to play in this by making sure they get their children vaccinated when sent reminder letters.'\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Rubella is an acute, contagious viral infection. While the illness is generally mild in children, it has serious consequences in pregnant women, causing fetal death or congenital defects known as congenital rubella syndrome (CRS). The rubella virus is transmitted by airborne droplets when infected people sneeze or cough. Humans are the only known host. In children, the disease is usually mild, with symptoms including a rash, low fever (under 39 C), nausea and mild conjunctivitis. The rash, which occurs in 50-80 percent of cases, usually starts on the face and neck before progressing down the body and lasts 1-3 days. Swollen lymph glands behind the ears and in the neck are the most characteristic clinical feature. Infected adults, more commonly women, may develop arthritis and painful joints that usually last from 3-10 days. Once a person is infected, the virus spreads throughout the body in about 5-7 days. Symptoms usually appear 2-3 weeks after exposure. The most infectious period is usually 1-5 days after the appearance of the rash.\n\nWhen a woman is infected with the rubella virus early in pregnancy, she has a 90 percent chance of passing the virus on to her fetus. This can cause miscarriage, stillbirth or severe birth defects known as CRS. Infants with CRS may excrete the virus for a year or more. Children with CRS can suffer hearing impairments, eye and heart defects and other lifelong disabilities, including autism, diabetes mellitus and thyroid dysfunction, many of which require costly therapy, surgeries and other expensive care.\n\nThe rubella vaccine is a live [naturally] attenuated strain that has been in use for more than 40 years. A single dose gives more than 95 percent long-lasting immunity, which is similar to that induced by natural infection. In the UK, rubella vaccine is usually administered as part of the triple MMR vaccine. The occurrence of rubella disease is an indication of less than adequate uptake of MMR vaccine. That apart, it is not clear why the incidence is currently so high in Hampshire.\n\nThe English county of Hampshire can be located in the interactive HealthMap at http://healthmap.org/r/1p4z. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "Parents have a really important role to play in this by making sure they get their children vaccinated when sent reminder letters.'\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Rubella is an acute, contagious viral infection. Although the infection can easily be prevented by youngsters receiving their measles, mumps and rubella (MMR) jabs, if caught, it can be dangerous, particularly to pregnant women, causing rubella-associated miscarriages and birth defects. In the UK, rubella vaccine is usually administered as part of the triple MMR vaccine. Latest figures have revealed that there have been 14 confirmed cases of the potentially dangerous infection in Hampshire and the Isle of Wight between January and July this year [2012] compared to none during the same period in 2011. The rubella virus is transmitted by airborne droplets when infected people sneeze or cough. A single dose gives more than 95 percent long-lasting immunity, which is similar to that induced by natural infection. The most infectious period is usually 1-5 days after the appearance of the rash. RUBELLA - UK: ENGLAND (HAMPSHIRE), ALERT\n", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "measles", "mumps", "rubella"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2011-03-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Hampshire", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "MMR2", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "the Isle of Wight", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "UK", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Southampton", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "1597149", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Rubella - Japan (03): vaccination policy", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=1597149", "date": "2013-03-21 13:10:42", "main_text": "RUBELLA - JAPAN (03): VACCINATION POLICY\n****************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Thu 21 Mar 2013\nFrom: M Ujtie <infinite_u@hotmail.com> [edited]\n\n\nA comment\n---------\nIn the past history of the National Immunization Program in Japan, MMR [measles mumps, rubella] vaccine was introduced for all children (including males) aged between 1 and 4 years old in April 1989, however the program was terminated in April 1993 due to a high frequency of reports of aseptic meningitis, a suspected side effect of the mumps vaccine (Urabe strain). So it is not true that male children were not administered rubella vaccine prior to 1995 in Japan.\n\nAlthough MMR vaccine was unfortunately discontinued in 1993, Japan had adopted the policy of vaccinating schoolgirls between 1977 and 1995, as several countries also did. As examples, United Kingdom (between 1970 and 1988), Australia (until 1983), Italy (until 1990), and The Netherlands (until 1987) had adopted a selective vaccination policy for girls.\n\nSince the beginning of 2013, a total of 1656 rubella cases have been reported predominantly among males aged between 20 and 40 years old in Japan. This number is still rapidly increasing and becoming close to last year's (2012) number of reported rubella cases (2353). A report mentioned that vaccination strategies that focused on women and children in Brazil were associated with a 5.5-fold greater incidence of rubella in men leading to new outbreaks and CRS [congenital rubella syndrome]. A combined vaccination strategy with a universal approach that included routine vaccination for boys, girls, women, and men should be globally promoted for preventing rubella outbreaks.\n\nThe original information sources for this comment include:\n- National Institute of Infectious Disease\nhttp://www.nih.go.jp/niid/images/idsc/disease/rubella/2013pdf/rube13-10.pdf (partly in Japanese)\n- J Child Psychol Psychiatry. 2005 Jun; 46(6): 572-9.\nhttp://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/?term=15877763\n- Vaccine. 2013 Mar 4. pii: S0264-410X(13)00236-3.\nhttp://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/?term=23470237\n- J R Coll Gen Pract. 1988 May; 38(310): 193-4.\nhttp://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/?term=3210177\n- Daily Mail\nhttp://www.dailymail.co.uk/health/article-17509/Why-Japan-banned-MMR-vaccine.html\n- Vaccines, 6th Edition, by Stanley A. RUBELLA - JAPAN (03): VACCINATION POLICY\n****************************************\n In the past history of the National Immunization Program in Japan, MMR [measles mumps, rubella] vaccine was introduced for all children (including males) aged between 1 and 4 years old in April 1989, however the program was terminated in April 1993 due to a high frequency of reports of aseptic meningitis, a suspected side effect of the mumps vaccine (Urabe strain). \nhttp://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/?term=15877763\n- Vaccine. The health authorities have issued the warning after a 1st case was recorded on Tue 8 May 2012 and 5 more a day later.\n\nOfficials say due to the airborne nature of the virus, containment is no longer an option. The Undersecretary for Health Improvement, Dr Cedric Alependava, says a nationwide vaccination program is being organised to start next month [June 2012].\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Transmitted in airborne droplets when infected people sneeze or cough, rubella is an acute, usually mild viral disease traditionally affecting susceptible children and young adults worldwide. Rubella infection just before conception and in early pregnancy may result in miscarriage, foetal death or congenital defects known as congenital rubella syndrome (CRS). The highest risk of CRS is found in countries with high rates of susceptibility to rubella among women of childbearing age.\n\nIn 1996, there were close to 13 000 cases in the WHO Western Pacific Region prior to the introduction of vaccines. Very few countries in these regions had introduced rubella-containing vaccine. Large-scale rubella vaccination during the last decade has drastically reduced or practically eliminated rubella and CRS in many developed and in some developing countries. Indeed, the western hemisphere and several European countries have eliminated rubella and CRS.\n\nThe WHO Measles and Rubella Initiative is backing a new 2012-2020 Measles & Rubella Strategic Plan. The strategic plan presents a 5-pronged strategy to cut global measles deaths by at least 95 percent by 2015 compared with 2000 levels and to achieve measles and rubella elimination in at least 5 WHO regions by 2020. The strategies include: High vaccination coverage; monitoring spread of disease using laboratory-backed surveillance; outbreak preparedness and response and case management; communication and community engagement; and research and development.\n\nSolomon Islands is a sovereign state in Oceania, east of Papua New Guinea, consisting of nearly 1000 islands. It covers a land mass of 28 400 square km. The capital, Honiara, is located on the island of Guadalcanal. Six fatal cases were reported during 1980 to 1994.\n- 3. 267 hospitalizations for rubella (0.065 per 100 000) were reported during 1997 to 2006, including 144 for intrpartum infection.\n- 4. Eighteen cases of congenital rubella syndrome were reported during the period 1997 through 2011.\n\nSeroprevalence surveys:\n\n- 95% of the general population of Madrid (98.6% of women of childbearing age) (2000 survey).\n- 98.1% in Catalonia (1996).\n- 96.4% of persons ages 17 to 36 (Barcelona, 1992 to 1993).\n- 91.2% of women of childbearing age (Aragon, 2003 to 2007).\n- 99.95% of pregnant women in Salamanca (2001).\n- 93.4% of pregnant women (94.9% of indigenous women, 89% of immigrants) at the time of delivery (Catalonia, 2007 publication).\n- 92% of pregnant immigrant women and 97.7% of native pregnant women (2006 to 2010)\n- 95% of native-born pregnant women (2007 to 2008).\n- 97.3% of pregnant women in Grenada (2007 to 2008).\n- 88.3% of immigrant women in Catalonia (2009 publication)\n- 95.7% of children ages 1 to 5 years, and 90.5% ages 5 to 1.4 in Castilla de Leon (2001 to 2002).\n\nNotable outbreaks:\n\n- 1991 - An outbreak (175 cases) was reported in Cadiz.\n- 2002 to 2003 - An outbreak was reported in Madrid; 19 cases (11 of whom were from Ecuador).\n- 2004 to 2005 - An outbreak (460 suspect cases) was reported in Madrid region.\n- 2005 - An outbreak (8 cases) was reported in Barcelona, among expatriates from Brazil.\n\nReferences:\n\n[1] Berger SA. Spain: (AN)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=1100360", "date": "2012-04-13 16:19:15", "main_text": "RUBELLA - SPAIN: (ARAGON)\n*************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Thu 12 Apr 2012\nSource: el Periodio Aragon [in Spanish, transl., edited by Mod.JG]\nhttp://www.elperiodicodearagon.com/noticias/aragon/un-brote-de-rubeola-en-una-guarderia-adelanta-vacunacion_748755.html\n\n\nTwo of the 17 cases of rubella detected in the past few days in Alcaniz occurred in a local nursery, and this led health care authorities to implement some measures, including bringing forward immunization dates with the measles, mumps and rubella (MMR) triple vaccine for children older than one year, until their 2 doses are completed.\n\nThis occurrence was reported in the most recent epidemiological bulletins issued by the Health and Sanitation Department of Aragon Government, corresponding to the epidemiological week between 26 Mar and 1 Apr 2012, also indicating that within the context of this alert, there are still 3 cases awaiting biological confirmation. 4 additional cases have been detected in Zaragoza.\n\nSince the last days of February, the Epidemiological Surveillance System in Aragon received information regarding 6 confirmed cases and 2 under investigation; including the cases from Alcaniz, and a single confirmed case and another under investigation in Zaragoza.\n\nAccording to the Aragon Government, all cases occurred in Romani heritage teenagers and young adults, but in none of them was there a history of recently traveling to their motherland [Romania?]. The Public Health authorities have applied control measures such as case isolation, contact identification, and assessment of their immune status, followed by vaccination if indicated, and separating pregnant women from potential contacts. Also, a campaign enforcing the administration of the triple MMR vaccine in the general population was implemented, particularly for persons with Romani heritage.\n\nAlso, during this period there were greater than expected numbers of influenza cases, although not reaching alert levels: 626 in Huesca, 558 in Teruel, and 3364 in Zaragoa. The Health Department decided to declare the end of the epidemic wave, since the incidence was below the epidemic threshold for 2 weeks in succession (10.5 cases per 100 000 inhabitants), but influenza surveillance will continue for a few more weeks in the Aragon Sentinel Network.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Aragon is the 4th (of 17) major region of Spain and makes up a 10th of the whole area of Spain. It has a population of some one and quarter million, but at a very low density (24 compared to the national average of 76 a square kilometre). The interactive HealthMap of Aragon can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/r/2bkL.\n\nRubella (also known as German measles) is a viral infection that used to be common in children. Rubella is usually a mild infection. Symptoms of rubella include a distinctive red-pink skin rash, swollen glands, cold-like symptoms such as a sore head and runny nose. The only time that rubella becomes a serious concern is if a pregnant woman catches the infection during the 1st 16 weeks of her pregnancy. This is because the rubella virus can disrupt the development of the baby and cause a wide range of health problems, such as: eye cataracts, deafness, heart abnormalities and brain damage. The birth defects caused by rubella virus are known as congenital rubella syndrome (CRS). Since the introduction of the mumps, measles and rubella (MMR) vaccine CRS is now rare in most developed communities.\n\nThe outbreak in Aragon appears to be affecting mainly members of the Romani community, an ethnic group living mostly in Europe, who trace their origins to the Indian Subcontinent. Romani are widely known in the English-speaking world by the exonym Gypsies. They are known collectively in the Romani language as Romane or Rromane (depending on the dialect concerned) and also as Romany, Romanies, Romanis, Roma or Roms. Romani are widely dispersed, with their largest concentrated populations in Europe, especially the Roma of Central and Eastern Europe and Anatolia, followed by the Kale of Iberia and Southern France. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "\n\n\nTwo of the 17 cases of rubella detected in the past few days in Alcaniz occurred in a local nursery, and this led health care authorities to implement some measures, including bringing forward immunization dates with the measles, mumps and rubella (MMR) triple vaccine for children older than one year, until their 2 doses are completed. The CHP\nreceived notification from Pamela Youde Nethersole Eastern Hospital\nand private doctors about 3 rubella cases involving Indonesian\ndomestic helpers, aged 27, 32 and 34, respectively. Laboratory tests\non the 34-year-old patient's blood samples yielded a positive result\nfor rubella virus [infection]. All of them are in stable condition. No\nhospitalisation is needed.\n\nUpon epidemiological investigation, CHP found that the trio attended\nthe same church before and during the onset of symptoms. An operation\nwas mounted today (Sun 16 Oct 2011) in which a health talk was\ndelivered. Questionnaires were distributed to attendees to assess\nwhether there were other persons with infection. A spokesman for the\nCHP said: 'Immunisation was offered to those in need. The CHP has\nidentified 11 people with symptoms and conducted blood tests on\nthem.'\n\nThe spokesman explained that rubella infection, also known as 'German\nMeasles,' is a highly infectious disease caused by rubella virus. It\ncan be transmitted by contact with secretions from nose and pharynx of\ninfected persons through droplet spread or direct contact with\npatients from one week before to at least 4 days after the onset of\nsymptoms. Incubation ranges from 14 to 23 days. As for symptoms,\nchildren usually present with a diffuse rash and enlargement of lymph\nnodes, but adults may experience a 1-5 day history of low-grade fever,\nheadache, malaise, mild cough and conjunctivitis followed by a skin\nrash as in children.\n\nRubella infection also produces anomalies in the developing fetus,\nleading to congenital rubella syndrome, which is characterised by\ndeafness, eye lesions, heart malformations and mental retardation in\ninfants born to women who were infected during the 1st 3 months of\npregnancy. The spokesman said that immunisation with rubella vaccine\nis effective in preventing the disease. He urged those who are not\nimmunised, especially child-bearing-aged women, to check their\nimmunity status before pregnancy and receive rubella vaccine\naccordingly.\n\n--\nCommunicated by;\nProMED-mail from HealthMap alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[By August 2006, 117 countries had implemented rubella vaccines as\npart of their routine national vaccination programmes. However, many\ncountries in Asia, such as the Philippines, Cambodia, Indonesia, and\nViet Nam, have not introduced rubella vaccination into their national\nimmunization program. Therefore, rubella infection remains endemic in\nmany countries in Asia, and immigrants from these countries pose a new\nchallenge in the global effort to decrease rubella infections.\n(Ching-Chiang Lin, et al., Am J Trop Med Hyg January 2010 vol. 82 no.\n1 40-44 http://www.ajtmh.org/content/82/1/40.full). It is not stated\nwhen the 3 Indonesian domestic workers arrived in Hong Kong, but it\nseems likely that they had not been immunised previously and\ncontracted infection in Hong Kong. If any of the women are pregnant,\nthe outcome could be serious. Further information is requested. -\nMod.CP]\n\n[For the interactive HealthMap/ProMED map of Hong Kong, see\nhttp://healthmap.org/r/1l5N. - Mod.MPP]", "summary": "\n\nRubella infection also produces anomalies in the developing fetus,\nleading to congenital rubella syndrome, which is characterised by\ndeafness, eye lesions, heart malformations and mental retardation in\ninfants born to women who were infected during the 1st 3 months of\npregnancy. Laboratory tests\non the 34-year-old patient's blood samples yielded a positive result\nfor rubella virus [infection]. is\ninvestigating a suspected outbreak of rubella involving 3 Indonesian\ndomestic helpers who attended a church in Causeway Bay.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "measles", "rubella"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2021-03-27 00:00:00", "2011-03-25 00:00:00", "2034-03-25 00:00:00", "2006-08-25 00:00:00", "2010-01-25 00:00:00", "2032-03-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Hong Kong", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Philippines", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Indonesia", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Viet Nam", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "HONG KONG", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Cambodia", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "1044148", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Rubella - Romania: (SJ) adolescents", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=1044148", "date": "2012-02-16 18:23:26", "main_text": "RUBELLA - ROMANIA: (SALAJ) ADOLESCENTS\n**************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Thu 16 Feb 2012\nSource: Eurosurveillance 2012; 17(7) [abbreviated & edited]\nhttp://www.eurosurveillance.org/ViewArticle.aspx?ArticleId=20089\n\n\nOngoing rubella outbreak among adolescents in Salaj, Romania, Sep 2011 to Jan 2012\n-------------------------------------\nBy: Janta D, Stanescu A, Lupulescu E, Molnar G, Pistol A. At: National Institute of Public Health, National Centre for Communicable Diseases Surveillance and Control, Bucharest, Romania, European Programme for Intervention Epidemiology Training (EPIET), European Centre for Disease Prevention and Control (ECDC), Stockholm, Sweden, National Institute of Research and Development for Microbiology and Immunology, Cantacuzino Reference Laboratory for Measles and Rubella, Bucharest, Romania, and Ministry of Health, Bucharest, Romania\n\nIntroduction\n----------\nA rubella outbreak has been ongoing in Salaj, Romania since September 2011 involving 1840 probable and confirmed cases among mainly unvaccinated adolescents. The index case became ill on 6 Sep 2011. The highest number of cases was recorded among 10-14 year olds and 15-19 year olds. Complications were recorded for 11 cases and included meningitis and arthritis. Although the peak has passed, surveillance is being maintained in the region.\n\nAn outbreak of rubella with more than 1800 cases was identified in Salaj, north western Romania, a district with 241 014 inhabitants in 2010. The European Union (EU) case definition [1] was used in the outbreak investigation, and 69 of the 1873 possible cases (3.6 per cent) were classified as laboratory-confirmed and 1771 (94.6 per cent) as probable, defined according to clinical criteria and epidemiological links with a confirmed case; 33 (1.8 per cent) were discarded. Of the 69 laboratory-confirmed cases, 2 were pregnant women, but no case of congenital rubella infection (CRI) has been reported so far.\n\nBackground\n----------\nRubella has been a statutorily notifiable disease in Romania since 1978 [2]. Until 2010, data were reported in aggregated format by age group. In 2010, case-based reporting with mandatory laboratory confirmation was introduced. Since December 2002, as part of the measles surveillance system, clusters of febrile rash are investigated by the local public health authorities and, in order to confirm the clinical diagnosis, it is recommended to take samples from 5-10 cases in each cluster for serological testing for measles and, if the results are negative, for rubella. If rubella transmission is confirmed, pregnant women who are epidemiologically linked to a laboratory-confirmed case or who meet the clinical criteria are given priority for testing and are informed about the potential risks of congenital rubella syndrome (CRS) to the foetus. Although rubella is usually a mild febrile rash illness, infection during pregnancy can lead to miscarriage, stillbirth, and birth defects associated with CRS, such as heart disease, blindness, deafness, and mental retardation.\n\nA national surveillance system for CRS was initiated in Romania in 2000. Newborns of rubella positive mothers have a rubella-specific IgM blood test tested for CRI and are reported in the CRI/CRS surveillance system according to the methodology in place [3]. Rubella is a vaccine preventable disease targeted for elimination in the WHO European Region by 2015 along with measles. Countries in the region have also committed to the prevention of CRS by the same year [4].\n\nA rubella-containing vaccine was 1st available in Romania in 1998 (bivalent measles-rubella), and it was offered to girls aged between 15 and 18 years (birth cohorts 1980-1983) as part of a mass vaccination campaign following a nationwide measles outbreak [5]. After a large rubella outbreak in 2002 to 2003, a rubella-containing vaccine was offered to girls aged between 13 and 14 years until 2008 (birth cohort 1994). In 2004, the measles-mumps-rubella (MMR) vaccine was introduced in the national immunisation schedule for children aged 12-15 months. Since 2004, MMR vaccination has also been offered to children aged 7 years.\n\nIn 2002 to 2003, a large rubella outbreak occurred in Romania, with more than 115 000 cases reported nationwide, and the highest incidence was reported in children of school age (2564 per 100 000 population aged 5-9 years and 2446 per 100 000 population aged 10-14 years) [6]. By 2010, rubella incidence dropped to 1.6 per 100 000 population (2009: 2.9/100 000; 2008: 8.1/100 000) [7,8].\n\nOutbreak description\n--------------------\nThe index case (laboratory-confirmed) was reported on 6 Sep 2011 to the Salaj Public Health Authority in an unvaccinated 16 year old student attending a local high school. Between 1 Sep 2011 and 23 Jan 2012, 1840 confirmed and probable rubella cases were reported by Salaj Public Health Authority.\n\nOf the 1840 cases 1069 were male, and 771 were female, showing a male:female ratio of 1.4:1. The highest number of cases was recorded in people aged between 10 and 19 years (n=1693). Of these, 1206 cases were registered among 15-19 year olds (58.6 per cent male and 41.4 per cent female) with a male:female ratio of 1.4:1. The rest of 487 cases were registered among 10-14 year olds (55.4 per cent male and 44.6 per cent female) with a male:female ratio of 1.2:1. Taking into account the fertile age, we registered 1341 cases in people aged between 15 and 44 years with a male:female ratio of 1.3:1. Among these 1341 cases, 59.1 per cent were male, and 40.9 per cent were female, giving a male:female ratio of 1.3:1. Of all outbreak cases, 23.3 per cent (428/1840) were registered among children born in 1996.\n\nThe incidence of rubella in Salaj was 763 per 100 000 population, with the highest incidence among high school teenagers aged between 15 and 19 years (9555 per 100 000 population for males and 7067 for females) followed by 10-14 year olds (3854 per 100 000 population for males, 3281 per 100 000 population for females). The 3rd most affected age group was the age group of 20-24 year olds with an incidence of 647 per 100 000 population among males and 154 per 100 000 population among females.\n\nComplications included meningitis (n=2 cases) and arthritis (n=9 cases). 35 cases required hospitalisation, and the median length of hospital stay was 4 days (minimum one, maximum 9). A total of 98 samples were tested for rubella IgM antibodies, and 69 of these were confirmed as positive. In clinical specimens from 2 cases, rubella virus genotype 2B was identified.\n\nVaccination coverage among the reported cases was low: 38 (2.1 per cent) of the total number of cases were vaccinated with one dose of rubella-containing vaccine. Taking into account the eligible birth cohorts for vaccination against rubella and the number of rubella cases reported during the outbreak, we calculated the proportion of rubella cases occurring in birth cohorts from 1989 to 2002. The highest proportion of cases was registered among people born from 1994 to 1997.\n\nWe looked at the rubella vaccination coverage for Romania and Salaj district in the eligible birth cohorts for MMR vaccination at 12-15 months (birth cohort 2004-2008), for MMR vaccination at 7 years (birth cohort 1998-2002), and the rubella dose administered only to girls in birth cohort 1989-2003. The rubella vaccination coverage of the 13 to 14 year old females in Salaj district during 2003 to 2008 is higher, with 3.8 per cent on average, than the coverage in the whole country for the same period. For 2005 to 2009, the MMR vaccination coverage in Salaj was below the country average, except for 7 year old children vaccinated with MMR in 2008 and 2010, when the district coverage was higher than the country average.\n\nControl measures\n----------------\nSince the identification of the outbreak, several control measures have been implemented by the local health authorities. The most important one was the initiation of a MMR vaccination campaign in the district of Salaj targeting all children and adolescents aged between 10 and 19 years, irrespective of their vaccination status. The MMR vaccine is supplied by the Ministry of Health and is offered free of charge through routine immunisation services (family physicians) and special outreach teams. As a result of this special campaign, 210 persons were vaccinated until 31 Dec 2011, but many parents still refused to have their children vaccinated. Local health care workers (doctors and nurses) were recommended to get vaccinated and to try to inform and increase awareness among their patients on the risk of this disease, especially among women of childbearing age.\n\nIn addition, the local public health authorities have initiated rubella screening among pregnant women epidemiologically linked to a probable or a confirmed case. While the general recommendations for MMR vaccination are maintained at national level, some districts bordering Salaj (Alba, Bihor, and Cluj) -- which were also affected by rubella, but to a lesser extent -- have issued vaccination recommendations among teenagers to avoid other potential outbreaks.\n\nDiscussion and conclusions\n--------------------------\nThe results of the investigation revealed that of the total number of rubella [cases] described above, 98 per cent had never been vaccinated against rubella infection. The index case was an unvaccinated teenager, and 1206 of the rubella cases (65.5 per cent) occurred among teenagers aged between 15 and 19 years (born between 1992 and 1996). Taking into account the historical MMR vaccination schedule in Romania, 770 children born between 1995 and 1996 (15-16 year olds in 2011) were not eligible for rubella vaccination and represent 41.8 per cent of all cases. In the birth cohort 1992-1994, only girls were eligible for rubella vaccine at the age of 14 years.\n\nMeasures such as catch up campaigns are important to close existing gaps in vaccination and prevent further spread of the outbreak. Educating the general public on modes of rubella transmission and stressing the need for rubella vaccination is the most important way to prevent further spread of the disease in other districts and to prevent congenital infection.\n\nAlthough the outbreak has passed its peak and is subsiding, surveillance for rubella will be maintained for a least 2 incubation periods (46 days) following onset of rash of the last case [9]. Moreover, active surveillance for infants with CRS will be carried out until the age of 9 months after the last reported rubella case [10].\n\n[Interested readers can view the references cited and the figures and tables by accessing the original text via the source URL. - Mod.CP]\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[It is clear that rubella virus infection remains a problem worldwide despite the existence of a secure vaccine and its generally cost-free availability. This appears to be due in part to continuing ignorance of the consequences of rubella infection in women of child-bearing age, and prejudice against the currently offered MMR (measles, mumps and rubella) triple vaccine. It might be beneficial to offer the alternative of a single component rubella vaccine where there is irreconcilable resistance to the MMR vaccine.\n\nThe Romanian county of Salaj lies in the area between the Apuseni Mountains and the Eastern Carpathians known as the Somes River Platform. It is located in the historical regions of Crisana and Transylvania, with the capital city at Zalau. A map of Salaj county can be accessed at http://www.aboutromania.com/maps39.html. - Mod.CP\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/r/1HU1.]", "summary": "In 2002 to 2003, a large rubella outbreak occurred in Romania, with more than 115 000 cases reported nationwide, and the highest incidence was reported in children of school age (2564 per 100 000 population aged 5-9 years and 2446 per 100 000 population aged 10-14 years) \n\nWe looked at the rubella vaccination coverage for Romania and Salaj district in the eligible birth cohorts for MMR vaccination at 12-15 months (birth cohort 2004-2008), for MMR vaccination at 7 years (birth cohort 1998-2002), and the rubella dose administered only to girls in birth cohort 1989-2003. Ministry spokesperson Peni Namotu told FBC News that there are\n11 confirmed cases of rubella -- most of which have been reported in\nSuva.\n\nRubella -- also known as German measles -- can cause serious birth\ndefects or miscarriage and is highly contagious. Namotu says the\nsymptoms of rubella include a mild fever, rash, runny nose, sore\nthroat and often swollen lymph nodes with aching joints. Namotu says\nthe treatment for symptoms of rubella include rest and plenty of\nfluids and if confirmed by the doctor then the person should stay at\nhome for 7 days.\n\nHe says rubella infection in pregnant women during the 1st 3 months of\npregnancy may result in the baby being born with birth defects or\ncongenital rubella syndrome. These birth defects include heart\ndefects, deafness, brain damage and eye problems, including\ncataracts.\n\n[Byline: Dev Sachindra]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[The number of confirmed cases of rubella virus infection in Fiji has\nincreased from the 3 (2 females and a male aged between 19 and 24)\nreported a week ago to the current total of 11 cases. It is not\nrevealed if any of the victims are pregnant women.\n\nCongenital rubella syndrome (CRS) is an important cause of severe\nbirth defects. When a woman is infected with the rubella virus early\nin pregnancy, she has a 90 percent chance of passing the virus on to\nher fetus. This can cause the death of the fetus, or it may cause CRS.\nEven though it is a mild childhood illness, CRS causes many birth\ndefects. Deafness is the most common, but CRS can also cause defects\nin the eyes, heart and brain. Rubella is spread in airborne droplets\nwhen infected people sneeze or cough. Once a person is infected, the\nvirus spreads throughout the body in about 5 to 7 days. During this\ntime, pregnant women may pass the virus on to their fetuses. Rubella\nis a vaccine-preventable disease.\n\nMore information on rubella and CRS and global statistics on rubella\nvaccine uptake (up to 2009) can be accessed at the WHO website\n(http://www.who.int/immunization_monitoring/diseases/rubella/en/index.html).\n\nThe HealthMap/ProMED-mail interactive map of Fiji can be accessed at\nhttp://healthmap.org/r/15-A. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "RUBELLA - FIJI (02)\n*******************\n\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n Namotu says\nthe treatment for symptoms of rubella include rest and plenty of\nfluids and if confirmed by the doctor then the person should stay at\nhome for 7 days.\n\n promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[The number of confirmed cases of rubella virus infection in Fiji has\nincreased from the 3 (2 females and a male aged between 19 and 24)\nreported a week ago to the current total of 11 cases. Northland medical officer of health Clair Mills said\nthe man was diagnosed with the rare disease after he visited a doctor\nlast week with a rash. The doctor decided to test for rubella and the\ndisease was confirmed by a laboratory. 3 people have been confirmed\nwith the illness in the past fortnight -- 2 in Auckland and the\nNorthland man. Only 30 confirmed cases have been recorded in New\nZealand in the past 11 years. Dr Mills said the man had no connection\nwith either of the 2 Auckland cases and he had not been in contact\nwith anyone who had been ill.\n\n'It's a real mystery where he got it from,' she said. 'It's a pretty\nrare disease and the main concern is around pregnant women because\nrubella can do pretty nasty things to the fetus.' Dr Mills said\nawareness of the recent measles outbreak in Auckland might have made\ndoctors more alert to the need to test for rubella, which is normally\na fairly mild illness for those other than pregnant women.\n\n'Sometimes the [rubella] rash isn't that significant and people don't\nthink it could be rubella so it's possible there may be more cases out\nthere,' she said. Northland health workers were checking who the man\nhad been in contact with.\n\nAlthough more than 100 people were confirmed with measles in the\nAuckland outbreak, so far there has been only one case in Northland --\na 9 year old boy from Hokianga. Meanwhile, a 3-year-old Ruakaka girl\nwho was taken to hospital with suspected measles last week did not\nhave the disease, tests showed.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nHealthMap Alerts via ProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Infection of the mother by rubella virus during pregnancy can be\nserious. If the mother is infected within the 1st 20 weeks of\npregnancy, the child may be born with congenital rubella syndrome\n(CRS), which entails a range of serious incurable illnesses.\nSpontaneous abortion occurs in up to 20 per cent of cases. The virus\nis transmitted via airborne droplets from the upper respiratory tract\nof active cases. She confirmed there were a total of 28\nconfirmed cases of rubella, of which 26 were males from the greater\nSuva area, and added that the ages of these victims ranged between 18\nto 32 years.\n\nRubella is an infectious viral illness of humans whose infections in\nchildren and adults are mostly mild, but an infection in women during\nthe early stages of pregnancy can cause serious birth defects or\nmiscarriage.\n\nThe infection is spread by coming into close contact with people who\nhave rubella, particularly if they are coughing or sneezing, and\nsymptoms include a mild fever, rash, runny nose, sore throat and often\nswollen lymph nodes. Aching joints are also common says Dr Saketa.\n\n'If a pregnant woman has a rash and fever or thinks she may have\nrubella, she should inform her clinic doctor as soon as possible. It\nis important to let the staff at the clinic know immediately so they\ncan keep you away from other pregnant women.'\n\nIn Fiji, women born after 1964 were immunised against rubella at 11\nyears of age, but from 2004 onwards, all children were immunised\nagainst rubella when they were one-year old and again with a combined\nmeasles-rubella vaccine when they reached class one. Dr Saketa says\nthe primary goal of the immunisation program is to prevent congenital\nrubella syndrome, as rubella infection in pregnant women during the\n1st 3 months of pregnancy can result in the baby being born with birth\ndefects or congenital rubella syndrome. Immunization is\nthe only way to protect against rubella virus infection, and women\nworking in health care settings are particularly vulnerable if\nunvaccinated. Since 2004, rubella vaccination has been universal in\nFiji, and cases of the disease have declined dramatically. The above\nreport indicates, however, that there are still sufficient\nunvaccinated individuals in the community to maintain the virus in\ncirculation. In\naddition, any resident of the Americas planning to travel to other\nregions of the world should be protected against measles and rubella\nprior to departing on their trip. Special efforts should be made to\nensure vaccination for women of childbearing age in order to prevent\npossible infections caused by the rubella virus during pregnancy.\n\nEvidence of immunity to measles and rubella for international\ntravelers includes:\n- written documentation of having received the measles and rubella\nvaccines\n- laboratory evidence of rubella and measles immunity (a positive\nserologic test for measles and rubella-specific IgG antibodies).\n\nTravelers over 6 months old who are unable to provide the indicated\ndocuments are advised to obtain vaccination for measles and rubella or\npreferably the MMR (measles, mumps, and rubella) vaccine. Ideally, the\nvaccine should be administered at least 2 weeks before departure.\n\nThere are exceptions to these instructions for travelers that present\nmedical indications that they should not undergo vaccination for\nmeasles and rubella. Infants under 6 months old should not be\nvaccinated. Infants that receive the MMR vaccine before their 1st\nbirthday must be revaccinated according to the vaccination schedule in\ntheir country.\n\nFor the duration of the trip and upon returning to their point of\norigin, travelers should take notice of the following symptoms:\n- Fever\n- Rash\n- Cough, coryza (runny nose), or conjunctivitis (red eyes).\n\nIf the traveler believes that they have measles or rubella, they\nshould do the following:\n- Remain in the place where they are lodging (hotel, home, etc.)\nexcept to go to a doctor. They should not travel nor go to any public\nplaces.\n- Avoid close contact with other people for 7 days following onset of\nrash.\n\nThe epidemiological surveillance system should include the private\nhealthcare sector and private healthcare facilities that provide\nmedical attention to tourists,since people who can afford to take\ninternational trips are more likely to seek medical attention in\nprivate healthcare facilities.\n\nTherefore, PAHO recommends alerting healthcare workers in both the\nprivate and public sectors to the possible presence of both diseases,\nand reminding them that such findings must be notified immediately in\norder to ensure a rapid response.\n\nIn addition to the measures already mentioned, PAHO encourages the\npractice of requiring proof of immunity for measles and rubella as a\npre-requisite to employment in the healthcare sector (medical,\nadministrative, and security personnel). Additionally, PAHO recommends\nthat personnel in the tourism and transportation sectors also be\nimmune to measles and rubella.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[According to PAHO (see ProMED-mail Measles, epidemiological alert,\nPAHO 20100903.3157), in 2002, endemic transmission of measles was\ninterrupted in the Americas, and in 2009, interruption of rubella's\nendemic transmission was achieved. Since 2003, historically low\nnumbers of measles cases -- both imported and import-related -- have\nbeen registered: 119 in 2003, 108 in 2004, 85 in 2005, 237 in 2006,\n167 in 2007, 207 in 2008 and 81 in 2009. In the 2008-2009 period, 199\nsecondary cases were registered as a result of a total of 69\nimportations, while the source for 24 cases were unknown. The notified\nmeasles cases reported in the Americas have been isolated or sporadic,\nand the outbreaks have resulted in limited secondary cases due to\nimportation. During [2010], measles cases, imported and\nimport-related, were registered in Argentina, Brazil, Canada, the USA\nand French Guiana. From January to 21 Aug 2010, 143 confirmed measles\ncases were registered, 40 of which were imported, 37 import-related\nand 66 of which were still under investigation or whose source of\ninfection was unknown.\n\nIn order to prevent the re-introduction of measles and rubella\nviruses in the region, the Pan American Health Organization (PAHO)\nencourages countries to continue observing the Technical Advisory\nGroup's (TAG) recommendations concerning surveillance, vaccination\nstrategies and laboratory-related issues (see\nhttp://new.paho.org/hq/index.php?option=com_content&task=view&id=1862&Itemid=358=en).\n- Mod.CP]", "summary": "\n\nEvidence of immunity to measles and rubella for international\ntravelers includes:\n- written documentation of having received the measles and rubella\nvaccines\n- laboratory evidence of rubella and measles immunity (a positive\nserologic test for measles and rubella-specific IgG antibodies).
