Of those, the\nmajority were from Viet Nam ... including 6 tourists. One CDC official\nsaid the weather in Viet Nam has not been normal this year [2011] ...\nand that the German measles season has started early. He noted that\nViet Nam already has in excess of 1000 cases.\n\nThe official is urging people traveling throughout Southeast Asia to\nmake sure they are vaccinated if they haven't previously caught the\ndisease. He said the government is also going to strictly enforce\nregulations passed in 2009 ... mandating that foreign laborers and\nexchange students must show immunization certificates before coming to\nTaiwan.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nHealthMap alerts via ProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Previously, it was reported in ProMED-mail (see: Rubella, adult -\nTaiwan 20110113.0152) that, according to surveillance data compiled by\nTaiwan CDC over recent years, about 20 to 50 rubella cases are\nreported each year, and several rubella outbreaks have occurred in\nfactories and at schools due to imported cases.\n\nThe incubation period of rubella is 14 days, with a range of 12 to 23\ndays. Symptoms are often mild, and up to 50 percent of infections may\nbe subclinical or unapparent. In children, rash is usually the 1st\nmanifestation, and a prodrome is rare. In older children and adults,\nthere is often a 1-5 day prodrome with low-grade fever, malaise,\nlymphadenopathy, and upper respiratory symptoms preceding the rash.\n\nThe rash of rubella is maculopapular and occurs 14 to 17 days after\nexposure. Arthralgia and arthritis occur frequently in adults and are\nconsidered to be an integral part of the illness rather than a\ncomplication. Other symptoms of rubella include conjunctivitis,\ntestalgia, or orchitis. Infection of the mother by rubella virus\nduring pregnancy can be serious. If the mother is infected within the\n1st 20 weeks of pregnancy, the child may be born with congenital\nrubella syndrome, which entails a range of serious incurable\nillnesses, and spontaneous abortion occurs in up to 20 percent of\ncases.\n\nRubella is spread from person to person via airborne transmission or\ndroplets shed from the respiratory secretions of infected persons.\nRubella may be transmitted by persons with subclinical or asymptomatic\ncases (up to 50 percent of all rubella virus infections). The\npreceding report suggests that rubella infection is seasonal in\nSoutheast Asia.\n\nRubella vaccine is generally available combined with measles and\nmumps vaccine (MMR vaccine). The rubella component of MMR vaccine is a\nlive attenuated virus. Vaccine virus is not communicable except in the\nsetting of breastfeeding. For further information, see:\nhttp://www.cdc.gov/vaccines/pubs/pinkbook/downloads/rubella.pdf.\n\nThe increasing incidence of rubella cases in Taiwan, although in part\ndue to infections acquired abroad, is indicative also of less than\noptimal MMR vaccine coverage in the indigenous population.\n\nThe HealthMap/ProMED-mail interactive map of Taiwan can be accessed\nat: http://healthmap.org/r/00DX. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "RUBELLA - TAIWAN\n****************\n\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n 1st 20 weeks of pregnancy, the child may be born with congenital\nrubella syndrome, which entails a range of serious incurable\nillnesses, and spontaneous abortion occurs in up to 20 percent of\ncases.\n\n He said the government is also going to strictly enforce\nregulations passed in 2009 ... mandating that foreign laborers and\nexchange students must show immunization certificates before coming to\nTaiwan. In most cases, rubella is a mild\ncondition, but it can be serious in pregnant women because it can harm\nthe unborn baby. On Mon\n[14 Feb 2011], 24 children were treated at the hospital for the\ndisease, taking the total number of affected children in the previous\nfew days to 30, he said. The hospital has isolated the patients to\navoid further transmission. It has also admitted 15 patients with\nviral fever and 9 others with serious dengue fever. The main reason\nfor the outbreaks was the abnormal weather, Ham added.\n\nIn Quang Nam Province, the General Hospital treated 90 people with\nrubella and viral fever in the [past] week. The disease is\nmild, but birth defects may occur if the infection is acquired by a\npregnant woman, resulting in deafness, cataracts, heart defects,\nmental retardation, and liver and spleen damage in 20 percent of cases\nif a woman is infected early in pregnancy\n\nRubella virus infection is spread by contact with an infected person,\nthrough coughing and sneezing. The average incubation period is 14\ndays with a range of 12-23 days. Rubella usually presents as a\nnonspecific, maculopapular, generalized rash lasting 3 days or fewer\nwith generalized lymphadenopathy, particularly of the posterior\nauricular, suboccipital and posterior cervical lymph nodes. Up to 50\npercent of infections occur without rash.\n\nMany illnesses can mimic rubella, and up to 50 percent of rubella\ninfections are asymptomatic. Therefore, the only reliable evidence of\nacute rubella virus infection is laboratory diagnosis. Rubella can be\nprevented by vaccination, and rubella has been largely eliminated\nwhere uptake of the MMR (measles, mumps and rubella) triple vaccine is\nhigh.\n\nThe Vietnamese report is unusual in that it implies that the\noccurrence of the disease is seasonal, localised, and synchronous with\noutbreaks of other viral fevers. Further information on the prevalence\nof rubella in Viet Nam would be appreciated.\n\nA map of the provinces of Viet Nam showing Da Nang and Quang Nam as\nadjacent provinces in the central region of Viet Nam is available at:\n<http://www.angelfire.com/co/hongnam/vnmap.html>.\n\nThe HealthMap/ProMED-mail interactive map of Viet Nam can be\naccessed: <http://healthmap.org/r/008c>. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "The disease is\nmild, but birth defects may occur if the infection is acquired by a\npregnant woman, resulting in deafness, cataracts, heart defects,\nmental retardation, and liver and spleen damage in 20 percent of cases\nif a woman is infected early in pregnancy\n\nRubella virus infection is spread by contact with an infected person,\nthrough coughing and sneezing. The case is a 40 year old man with no\nrecent travel history. On 18 Dec 2010, he developed symptoms such as fever,\nrash and headache. Taiwan CDC later confirmed German measles infection in\nthe case.\n\nDuring the investigation into the source of his infection, personnel from\nTaiwan CDC discovered that a 44 year old woman who resides in the same\ndistrict had developed similar symptoms in mid-November [2010]. When she\nsought medical attention, she was diagnosed with an upper respiratory tract\ninfection. Taiwan CDC also later confirmed rubella infection in her.\nAlthough she travelled to Japan during early November [2010], [the CDC] was\nnot able to determine where she became infected with rubella as she was\ntravelling back and forth between Taiwan and Japan when she developed her\ninfection.\n\nDue to the difference in their disease onset times, they are not directly\nrelated even though both cases reside in the same district. The local\nhealth bureau has already compiled a list of close contacts and is closely\nmonitoring the close contacts. As of now, no suspected case has been reported.\n\nGerman measles, also known as rubella, is caused by the rubella virus. It\nis transmitted through airborne droplet or close contact. The symptoms\ninclude fever, conjunctivitis, nasopharyngitis, swollen glands\n(suboccipital and posterior cervical lymphadenopathy), and rash. If a\npregnant woman becomes infected with German measles during the first 3\nmonths of her pregnancy, there is a 90 per cent chance she will pass the\ninfection to her unborn child and a 25 per cent chance that the child will\ndevelop congenital rubella syndrome, resulting in fetal death, miscarriage,\nor birth defects. Hence, countries around the world work hard to eliminate\nthe disease.\n\nAccording to the surveillance data compiled by Taiwan CDC over the recent\nyears, about 20 to 50 German measles cases are reported each year and\nseveral German measles outbreaks have occurred in factories and at schools\ndue to imported cases. Taiwan still faces the threat of rubella as there\nare ongoing outbreaks in neighboring countries such as China and those in\nSouth East Asia.\n\nThe best way to prevent German measles is vaccination. In Taiwan, the\nexisting routine childhood vaccination schedule recommends a dose of MMR\n(measles, mumps, and rubella) vaccine to children 12 months of age and\nanother dose to 1st graders in elementary schools. Taiwan CDC once again\nreminds the public the importance of timely vaccination for children. Women\nof child-bearing age who are tested negative for rubella antibody during\nthe pre-marital medical examination should receive the MMR vaccine. If\npregnancy occurs before vaccination, avoid crowded public places during\npregnancy to lower the chance of infection.\n\nAbout 25 to 50 per cent of German measles cases occur without symptoms.\nFurther, cases develop symptoms that are similar to those of other\ninfectious and non-infectious diseases. Taiwan CDC reminds all physicians\nto remain vigilant for suspected cases of German measles and report\nsuspected cases that are not vaccinated against German measles and have\ntravelled to endemic areas prior to disease onset to the local health\nbureau immediately for the health authorities to take prompt actions that\nprevent further transmission of the disease.\n\nEmployers who hire foreign workers and schools with foreign students are\nadvised to reinforce health promotion programs and messages. If workers or\nstudents develop suspected symptoms of German measles, employers or schools\nshould assist them in seeking medical attention and report the cases to the\nlocal health authority to prevent further spread of the disease. For more\ninformation, please visit the Taiwan CDC's website: <http://www.cdc.gov.tw/>.\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nHealthMap Alerts via\nProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[An astute piece of diagnosis revealing that measles and mumps infections\nare not the only detrimental consequences of deficits in MMR vaccine\ncoverage. The detection of rubella virus infection in unrelated adults in\nTaiwan signals the continued transmission of rubella virus in the community\nand reinforces the need to achieve universal MMR vaccination, there and\nelsewhere.\n\nThe HealthMap/ProMED-mail interactive map of Taiwan can be accessed at\n<http://healthmap.org/r/00DX>. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "For more\ninformation, please visit the Taiwan CDC's website: <http://www.cdc.gov.tw/>.\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nHealthMap Alerts via\nProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[An astute piece of diagnosis revealing that measles and mumps infections\nare not the only detrimental consequences of deficits in MMR vaccine\ncoverage. Rubella is a viral infection,\ncommonly known as German Measles.\n\nIt is generally a mild illness, but can cause birth defects if a\nwoman catches the disease in early pregnancy. The main symptoms are a\nfever and red-brown rash. Swollen glands, a lack of energy and aches\nand pains are also common. The disease is passed on through droplets\nin the air from coughs and sneezes of infected people.\n\nDr. Julie Cavanagh, from the National Health Service Tayside, said:\n'The health protection team is concerned that rubella infection may\nbe circulating in the local area. However, I would stress that the\nmajority of people are actually immune to the disease. The safest way\nto protect yourself and your children against measles, mumps and\nrubella is the combined MMR [measles, mumps and rubella] triple\nvaccine and I would encourage all parents to ensure their children's\nvaccinations are up to date.'\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail Rapporteur Brent Barrett\n\n[Crieff is a small town in a semi-rural area of central Scotland. It\ncan be located just to the west of Perth in the HealthMap/ProMED-mail\ninteractive map of the United Kingdom at:\n<http://healthmap.org/promed?v=54.5,-2,5>. Rubella infection is\nlikely to be another consequence of low uptake of the MMR triple\nvaccine. It is not immediately obvious, however, why uptake of the\nvaccine might be lower than elsewhere in Scotland. It may be that\nthis region has been at greater risk of exposure to infection as a\nconsequence of the presence of seasonal agricultural workers. The estimated total size of this population is 300 000 people,\nbut the number of families that totally object to vaccination is\ndecreasing. An earlier rubella epidemic occurred in this community in the\nmid-1990s. It is difficult to estimate the number of susceptibles among\nthis community.\nThere were 7 cases reported in individuals not vaccinated for 'other than\nreligious objection'. We have an increasing number of 'critical' parents,\nsimilar to the antivaccination lobby. No cases were reported from that type\nof non-vaccinated individuals.\nRubella surveillance is laboratory-based, however, so most cases are not\nreported, because they are never lab-confirmed. Underreporting by labs is\nzero. With large numbers of asymptomatic infections, and large numbers of\nnot confirmed symptomatic cases, we are doing some guesswork on the real\nsituation.\n--\nSusan Hahne\nCentre for Infectious Disease Control\nRIVM, Bilthoven,\nThe Netherlands\n<Susan.Hahne@rivm.nl>\nand\nJim van Steenbergen\nLCI (National Coordinator Communicable Disease Control)\nPOBox 85300, NL-3508 AH\nUtrecht\nThe Netherlands\n<jvansteenbergen.lci@ggd.nl>\n[ProMED-mail would like to thank Drs Hahne and van Steenbergen for their\nprompt contribution, giving the background on the rubella outbreak in the\nNetherlands that appears to be related to the reported outbreak of rubella\nin Canada. Most of the patients are from Cu Chi District, and they work in\nindustrial parks in the city, the local newspaper Labor reported on\nFri 18 Mar 2005.\nTo deal with the situation, local health agencies are strengthening\nvaccination among residents, especially young women at universities\nand factories, and children aged 12-24 months. They are also urging\npeople to improve hygiene in their living and working environments.\nGerman measles, also known as rubella, or 3-day measles, is caused by\na virus that is spread through the air or by close contact. It can\nalso be transmitted to a fetus by a mother with an active infection,\ncausing severe disease in the fetus. In children and adults, rubella\nis usually mild and may even go unnoticed.\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[Rubella is endemic world-wide. It is a vaccine-preventable disease,\nand vaccination is universal in most developed countries.\nConsequently, ProMED-mail rarely reports outbreaks of rubella. The\ncurrent outbreak in Ho Chi Minh City attracts attention because of\nits extent and the age groups affected, which suggest either a\nbreakdown in vaccine coverage or an inadequacy of the vaccine\nemployed. Unlike many other infectious disease problems afflicting\nViet Nam at the present time, no vector or alternate host is\ninvolved. (_Rubella virus_ is the sole representative of the genus\n_Rubivirus_ of the family _Togaviridae_). The disease rubella, or\nGerman measles, is generally benign in children and adults.\nCongenital transmission is the most serious consequence of rubella\nvirus infection, and virus infection of the fetus during the 1st\ntrimester of pregnancy can result in a constellation of serious\nimmunological and neurological birth defects known collectively as\nthe congenital rubella syndrome. In view of the involvement in Ho Chi\nMinh City of a large number of young adult women, it will be\nadvisable for the local health-care authorities to undertake\nsurveillance for congenital rubella syndrome in the wake of such an\noutbreak. - Mods.MPP/CP]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n Congenital transmission is the most serious consequence of rubella\nvirus infection, and virus infection of the fetus during the 1st\ntrimester of pregnancy can result in a constellation of serious\nimmunological and neurological birth defects known collectively as\nthe congenital rubella syndrome. RUBELLA - RUSSIA (VOLGOGRAD)\n", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "measles", "mumps", "rubella"], "syndromes": ["encephalitis"], "event_date": ["2021-03-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "russia", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2207901", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Rubella - Canada ex Netherlands (03)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2207901", "date": "2005-05-20 23:50:00", "main_text": "RUBELLA - CANADA EX NETHERLANDS (03)\n************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\nDate: Thu 19 May 2005\nFrom: ProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\nSource: Eurosurveillance Weekly, Vol 10, Issue 20, Thu 19 May 2005 [edited]\n<http://www.eurosurveillance.org/ew/2005/050519.asp>\n\nRubella Outbreak in an Unvaccinated Religious Community in The Netherlands\nSpreads to Canada\n---------------------------------------------------\nThere are indications that the rubella outbreak that started in September\n2004 among members of a religious community in the Netherlands, first\nreported in Eurosurveillance on 3 Mar 2005 (1), has spread to Canada. This\noutbreak is specifically affecting some unvaccinated groups within the\nGereformeerde Gemeente in Nederland (Reformed Community in the Netherlands,\na Christian community).\nUp to Tue 17 May 2005, 214 laboratory confirmed cases of rubella have been\nreported in southwest Ontario. 5 of these cases have been in pregnant\nwomen. The Canadian Christian community where the cases occurred has\nhistorical and social links with the affected groups within the\nGereformeerde Gemeente in Nederland, and individuals frequently travel\nbetween the 2 communities. A definitive source for the Canadian patient\nwith the earliest date of onset reported (mid-February 2005) has not yet\nbeen identified. An isolate has been obtained from the outbreak in Canada\nand is currently being genotyped. Attempts are being made to isolate\nrubella virus in the Netherlands so as to genetically link the respective\noutbreaks. The World Health Organization has recently published a\nstandardised rubella virus genotyping protocol (2).\nIn the Netherlands, up to 17 May 2005, 309 laboratory confirmed cases have\nbeen reported (from 1 Sep 2004); 23 of these are known to be in pregnant\nwomen (9 in their 1st trimester). The geographical spread of the outbreak\nin the Netherlands is documented on the Rijksinstituut voor Volksgezondheid\nen Milieu website: <http://www.rivm.nl/vtv/object_class/atl_infparasit.html>.\nThe epidemiological curve, shown as a figure entitled: ' Reports of\nlaboratory confirmed cases of rubella by week of onset, the Netherlands\n(cases reported between 1 September 2004 - 17 May 2005. Source: Osiris.')\nin the original document shows 3 separate peaks, each larger than the last.\nIt is uncertain whether these peaks reflect the true incidence or are the\nresult of a registration artefact.\nIn both the Netherlands and Canada, the outbreak occurred in a community\nwith low measles, mumps, and rubella (MMR) vaccination coverage and strong\nsocial adherence. The proportion of cases in a vaccinated individual is low\n(0.3 percent and 0.6 percent, respectively), indicating that the\neffectiveness of the rubella component of the MMR vaccine is very high.\nRubella infection acquired during early pregnancy can lead to miscarriage\nor severe birth defects known as congenital rubella syndrome (CRS). This\nsyndrome occurs in up to 90 percent of infants born to mothers who are\ninfected in the 1st trimester (3). It is important to differentiate primary\nrubella infection from re-infection because the risk of CRS for\nre-infection during the first trimester of pregnancy is less than 5 to 10\npercent (2). Rubella IgG avidity serology has been shown to be a very\nuseful laboratory test for differentiating primary rubella infections from\nre-infections/past infections in pregnant women where critical patient\nmanagement/counselling decisions are required (4). Rubella IgG avidity\nserology is being used for the investigation of rubella exposure or\nsuspected rubella in pregnant women in the Canadian outbreak.\nRubella and CRS are preventable by immunisation, and both the Netherlands\nand Canada have a routine 2-dose MMR vaccination schedule. During the\noutbreak, health authorities in Canada and the Netherlands have offered MMR\nfree of charge to unvaccinated individuals (in the Netherlands this has\nonly been offered to those under the age of 18). Public health laws in\nOntario allow authorities to exclude unvaccinated children from attending\nschool when there is an outbreak of a vaccine preventable disease. Local\npublic health officials in Ontario have issued indefinite exclusion orders\nfor students who are not immunised or cannot offer proof of immunity. Such\na law does not exist in the Netherlands. In addition, the provincial\nMinistry of Health for Ontario (Ontario Ministry of Health and Long Term\nCare) has given advice on isolation of cases, quarantining of contacts and\ntravel restrictions. This also differs from the Netherlands, where the\nemphasis of public health advice is on advising pregnant women to avoid\ncontact with rubella patients.\nThe effectiveness of both Canadian and Dutch public health advice in\npreventing spread of rubella and in preventing pregnant women from becoming\ninfected is probably limited. This is firstly because members of the\naffected communities often decline vaccination, since it contradicts their\nreligious beliefs. Comprehensive information on the uptake of MMR during\nthis outbreak is not yet available in Canada or the Netherlands. Secondly,\nrubella is most infectious prior to the onset of rash (usual range one week\nprior to 4 days post rash onset). Finally, only a minority of cases are\ndiagnosed, since rubella virus infection can be asymptomatic in up to 50\npercent of cases and, if symptomatic, usually has a mild course.\nFurther spread of the outbreak and risk of CRS depends on herd immunity\n(resulting from vaccination and natural infection) and level of contact\nwith the affected community. In the Netherlands, historical seroprevalence\nand vaccine uptake data suggest that the level of protection in the general\npopulation is high (5). Even in municipalities where a high proportion of\nthe population declines vaccination, seroprevalence studies suggest\nthat >97 percent of women of childbearing age are immune (probably through\ncirculation of rubella virus in the past) (5). In addition to the groups\nwithin the Gereformeerde Gemeente in Nederland, Dutch groups with a\nrelatively low seroprevalence may include some groups of immigrants and\nthose supporting the anti-vaccination movement (including followers of\nhomeopathy and anthroposophical teachings).\nIn Canada, populations with relatively low seroprevalence may include\nimmigrants as well as other groups who resist immunisation for religious\nand philosophical reasons.\nAlthough Reformed Christian communities exist outside the Netherlands and\nCanada, to our knowledge vaccine preventable diseases have only spread\ninternationally from the Netherlands Gereformeerde Gemeente in the\nNederlands to Canada (6,7,8). Canada's temperate climate, which has\nsynchronous seasons to those of the Netherlands, may be one explanation for\nthis. However, onward spread from Canada has been documented in the past:\nthe poliomyelitis outbreak in the Netherlands in 1978 spread to Canada and\nsubsequently into the United States (9).\nPublic health efforts in the Netherlands and Canada are now focusing on\nraising awareness amongst the affected community and health professionals,\ndocumenting (molecular) epidemiological links, and improving surveillance\nof CRS.\nReferences:\n-----------\n(1) Hahne S, Ward M, Abbink F, Binnendijk R van, Ruijs H, Steenbergen J\nvan, Timen A and Melker H de. Large ongoing rubella outbreak in religious\ncommunity in the Netherlands since September 2004. Eurosurveillance Weekly\n2005: 10(3): 03/03/2005.\n(<http://www.eurosurveillance.org/ew/2005/050303.asp#3>)\n(2) WHO. Standardization of the nomenclature for genetic characteristics of\nwild-type rubella viruses. Weekly Epidemiological Record 2005;14:126-132.\n(3) Banatvala JE, Brown DW. Rubella. Lancet 2004;363(9415):1127-37.\n(4) Best JM, O'Shea S, Tipples G, Davies N, Al-Khusaiby S, Krause A,\nHesketh L, Jin L, Enders G. Interpretation of rubella serology in pregnancy\n- pitfalls and problems. BMJ 2002;325:147-8.\n(5) Haas R de, Hof S van den, Berbers GA, Melker HE de, Conyn-van\nSpaendonck MA. Prevalence of antibodies against rubella virus in The\nNetherlands 9 years after changing from selective to mass vaccination.\nEpidemiol Infect. 1999;123(2):263-70.\n(6) Drebot MA, Mulders MN, Campbell JJ, Kew OM, Fonseca K, Strong D et al.\nMolecular detection of an importation of type 3 wild poliovirus into Canada\nfrom The Netherlands in 1993. Appl Environ Microbiol 1997;63(2):519-23.\n(7) Hof S van den, Meffre CM, Conyn-van Spaendonck MA, Woonink F, Melker HE\nde, Binnendijk RS van. Measles outbreak in a community with very low\nvaccine coverage, the Netherlands. Emerg Infect Dis 2001;7 (3 Suppl):593-7.\n(8) Furesz J, Armstrong RE, Contreras G. Dev Biol Stand.\n1981;47:307-15.\n[Byline: Susan Hahne (<susan.hahne@rivm.nl>)1, Jeannette Macey2, Graham\nTipples2, Paul Varughese2, Arlene King2, Rob van Binnendijk1, Helma\nRuijs1,3, Jim van Steenbergen1, Aura Timen1, Anton M van Loon4 and Hester\nde Melker1\n1) Centre for Infectious Disease Control, Rijksinstituut voor\nVolksgezondheid en Milieu (RIVM), the Netherlands\n2) Public Health Agency of Canada (PHAC), Canada\n3) Municipal Health Authority (GGD) Rivierenland, the Netherlands\n4) Department of Virology, University Medical Centre (UMC) Utrecht, the\nNetherlands]\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[The following extract from a recent MMWR report on Elimination of Rubella\nand Congenital Rubella Syndrome in the United States, 1969--2004, puts the\nsituation in Canada in perspective.\n(<http://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/mm5411a5.htm>)\n'The changing epidemiology of rubella in the United States during the\npreceding 10 years reflected efforts to control the disease elsewhere in\nthe Western Hemisphere. The burden of rubella was increasingly recognized\nin countries as their vaccination programs succeeded in controlling\nmeasles, whose symptoms can resemble rubella. In 1997, a Technical Advisory\nGroup for the Pan American Health Organization (PAHO) recommended\nstrategies for rubella control and CRS prevention. In 2003, with the\nsuccess of accelerated rubella control, PAHO member countries voted to\nestablish a goal to eliminate rubella and CRS from the Western Hemisphere\nby 2010. As of 2004, a total of 43 of 44 countries and territories in the\nregion had included rubella vaccine in their routine immunization programs.\nFor countries reporting rubella cases to PAHO, the number of reported\nrubella cases dropped from 135 947 in 1998 to fewer than 1000 cases in 2003.\nWith elimination of endemic chains of rubella transmission in the United\nStates, future patterns of rubella will most likely reflect global disease\nepidemiology. Since 1998, most non-U.S.-born cases of rubella reported in\nthe United States have occurred among persons born in countries where\nrubella vaccination has not been or was only recently implemented.\nAccording to a survey of the member countries in the World Health\nOrganization, the number of countries that have incorporated\nrubella-containing vaccine into their routine national immunization\nprograms increased from 65 (33 percent) in 1996 to 110 (57 percent) in\n2003. However, rubella continues to be endemic in many parts of the world.\nThe United States should continue its vigilance against rubella and CRS by\n1) maintaining high vaccination rates among children; 2) ensuring\nvaccination among women of childbearing age, especially women born outside\nthe United States; 3) continuing surveillance of both rubella and CRS; and\n4) responding rapidly to any outbreak.'\n- Mod.CP]", "summary": "Public Health Agency of Canada (PHAC), Canada\n3) Municipal Health Authority (GGD) Rivierenland, the Netherlands\n4) Department of Virology, University Medical Centre (UMC) Utrecht, the\nNetherlands]\n--\nProMED-mail\n< A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n The United States should continue its vigilance against rubella and CRS by\n1) maintaining high vaccination rates among children; 2) ensuring\nvaccination among women of childbearing age, especially women born outside\nthe United States; 3) continuing surveillance of both rubella and CRS; and\n4) responding rapidly to any outbreak.' [The following extract from a recent MMWR report on Elimination of Rubella\nand Congenital Rubella Syndrome in the United States, 1969--2004, puts the\nsituation in Canada in perspective. In both the Netherlands and Canada, the outbreak occurred in a community\nwith low measles, mumps, and rubella (MMR) vaccination coverage and strong\nsocial adherence. The effectiveness of both Canadian and Dutch public health advice in\npreventing spread of rubella and in preventing pregnant women from becoming\ninfected is probably limited. In the Netherlands, up to 17 May 2005, 309 laboratory confirmed cases have\nbeen reported (from 1 Sep 2004); 23 of these are known to be in pregnant\nwomen (9 in their 1st trimester). This also differs from the Netherlands, where the\nemphasis of public health advice is on advising pregnant women to avoid\ncontact with rubella patients. Secondly,\nrubella is most infectious prior to the onset of rash (usual range one week\nprior to 4 days post rash onset). Further spread of the outbreak and risk of CRS depends on herd immunity\n(resulting from vaccination and natural infection) and level of contact\nwith the affected community. (4) Best JM, O'Shea S, Tipples G, Davies N, Al-Khusaiby S, Krause A,\nHesketh L, Jin L, Enders G. Interpretation of rubella serology in pregnancy\n- pitfalls and problems. In 1997, a Technical Advisory\nGroup for the Pan American Health Organization (PAHO) recommended\nstrategies for rubella control and CRS prevention. Attempts are being made to isolate\nrubella virus in the Netherlands so as to genetically link the respective\noutbreaks.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "measles", "mumps", "poliomyelitis", "rubella"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["1969-03-25 00:00:00", "1997-03-25 00:00:00", "1998-03-25 00:00:00", "1996-03-25 00:00:00", "1979-03-25 00:00:00", "1993-03-25 00:00:00", "2004-03-25 00:00:00", "1978-03-25 00:00:00", "2005-05-17 00:00:00", "2004-09-25 00:00:00", "2003-03-25 00:00:00", "2010-03-25 00:00:00", "2005-05-25 00:00:00", "2004-09-01 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "the Netherlands", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Nederland", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "the Gereformeerde Gemeente", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "O'Shea S", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Heyward JT", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "the \n Nederlands", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Netherlands", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Ontario", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "eurosurveillance", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "the United States", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Hof S", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "the \n Netherlands", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Canada", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "the United \n States", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2207837", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Rubella - Canada ex Netherlands", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2207837", "date": "2005-05-10 23:50:00", "main_text": "RUBELLA - CANADA EX NETHERLANDS\n*******************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n[1]\nDate: 10 May 2005\nFrom: ProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\nSource: Canada.com [edited]\n<http://www.canada.com/fortstjohn/story.html?id=955fdd33-6825-402a-a797-0bdbef1d4efd>\n\nRubella cases probably from Netherlands\n---------------------------------------\nThe rubella outbreak galloping through a cluster of religious communities\nin south western Ontario probably originated in a outbreak in the\nNetherlands, Ontario's chief medical officer of health said on Monday [9\nMay 2005].\nDr Sheela Basrur confirmed that a 3rd pregnant woman has tested positive\nfor the disease, which can trigger a miscarriage or cause birth defects in\nthe fetus when contracted in the early stages of pregnancy. 'We are\nspeculating that it was introduced from the Netherlands, given the\ncross-border traffic that exists between this community and a similar\ncommunity in the Netherlands,' Basrur said. 'They've had an outbreak going\non for 8 months now in what they call the Bible Belt.' Ontario's health\nminister predicted the outbreak could drag on for some weeks yet.\n'We're really still in the phase of the outbreak where our investigations\nwill lead to more people (with the virus),' George Smitherman said. 'We're\nnot at a point yet where we can declare the outbreak under control.' Basrur\nsaid to date there is no evidence that the virus has moved into other\nnearby religious groups that also shun vaccination. But health officials\nare investigating whether there are any links between the communities and\nwhether additional measures are needed to reduce the chance of it spreading.\nMeanwhile, Basrur said she'll be pressing members of all the religious\ngroups to have their children vaccinated if they are currently unprotected.\n'We have not yet seen spread to the Amish or Mennonite communities. (But)\nthey do live in adjacent geographic areas. And so for that reason, those\nhealth units in which they live will be redoubling their efforts to\nencourage them to seek vaccination,' she said.\nAs of Monday the case count in the outbreak was 121, including the 3\npregnant women. 7 other pregnant women who may have been exposed to the\nhighly contagious virus are under observation.\nRubella poses its greatest risk to fetuses in the first 11 weeks of\ngestation. Children born with congenital rubella syndrome can suffer\ncognitive disabilities or may be deaf or blind. Citing confidentiality\nrules, Basrur wouldn't say at what point in their pregnancy these women are.\nCuriously, the case count also includes a 32 year old man who was\nvaccinated against rubella in 1974. The man attends Westervelt community\ncollege in nearby London, Ontario. Public health authorities urged any\npregnant women at the college to contact their physicians immediately.\nBasrur said it's suspected that this case of vaccine failure was probably\ncaused by a break in what's called the cold chain -- the requirement to\nensure vaccines are stored at temperatures no higher than 8 degrees Celsius\nuntil they are administered.\nIt is believed the man became infected after coming in contact with fellow\nstudents who are older siblings of children attending the religious school\nthat has been at the heart of the outbreak -- Rehoboth Christian School in\nNorwich, about 40 kilometres east of London. The school draws its students\nfrom 4 closely aligned religious denominations: the Heritage Netherlands\nReformed Congregation, the Old Reformed Congregation, the Netherlands\nReformed Congregation of North America, and the Netherlands Reformed\nCongregation. The bulk of the school's 651 students come from this last\nchurch, which is officially neutral on the issue of vaccination. But many\nof its members choose not to immunize their children, and about 60 per cent\nof the students attending Rehoboth Christian School are unvaccinated.\nPublic health authorities have issued an indefinite school exclusion order\nfor unvaccinated children who have never had rubella, which is also known\nas German measles. Rubella is a viral illness spread through coughing or\nsneezing. Its symptoms include rash, fever, swollen glands, joint pain,\nmuscle pain and runny nose. Public health officials hope to eliminate the\nvirus in the Americas by 2010 but have recognized that while the virus\ncirculates elsewhere, eradication here is unlikely.\n[byline: Helen Branswell]\n******\n[2]\nDate: 10 May 2005\nFrom: ProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\nSource: Canada.com [29 Apr 2005] [edited]\n<http://www.canada.com/health/story.html?id=28cc66e0-c833-4aaf-a22f-8e7472fafbca>\n\nOntario reports large rubella outbreak, warns could be tip of iceberg\n-----------------------\nPublic health officials in Ontario reported a large outbreak of rubella,\nsometimes known as German measles, on Friday [29 Apr 2005] and warned the\n50 cases that have currently been confirmed could be the tip of the iceberg.\nDr Sheela Basrur, the province's chief medical officer of health,\nconfirmed the cluster centres on a school in the Woodstock region in Oxford\nCounty, but has spilled into the neighbouring counties of Brant and\nNorfolk. The known cases are mainly young children at this point, she said\nin an interview from Kingston.\n'The cases appear to be linked to the Dutch Reform Church which tends to\navoid immunization for religious reasons,' Basrur said. 'Therefore we have\na highly susceptible population that are unimmunized against common\nchildhood diseases, including German measles.'\n[byline: Helen Branswell]\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[Rubella is a vaccine-preventable disease, still endemic in many countries\n(see below -- a total of 10 prior postings on rubella in over 10 years of\nProMED-mail's existence). However, this outbreak has too many 'deja vu'\ncomponents to allow this moderator to follow our policy of not posting\n'routine' outbreaks of vaccine-preventable diseases -- and definitely\nserves as a reminder that we live in a global village.\nIn 1978/1979, the last outbreak of polio in Canada and the USA was related\nto travel between the Netherlands (where the outbreak was ongoing in 1978)\nand Canada by members of the Reformed Netherlands Congregation, a religious\ngroup that refused vaccinations based on religious beliefs. The following\nquote is from a MMWR article originally published on 27 Jul 1979 and\ncontributed to by this moderator at that time: 'Epidemiologic information\nalso links last year's [1978] poliomyelitis epidemic in the Netherlands and\nCanada with this year's outbreak in the United States and Canada. During\nthe 1978 outbreak, members of the affected religious group traveled from\nthe Netherlands to Canada, where cases subsequently appeared. An Amish\nfamily from an Ontario town [near Woodstock] 15 miles from the affected\narea moved in late summer 1978 to the Pennsylvania town where the 1st U.S.\nAmish case subsequently occurred, in January 1979. There were also other,\nless well-defined contacts between Amish persons in Ontario and\nPennsylvania.' <http://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/00050435.htm>\nGenetic markers performed on the viruses involved in all 3 countries were\nsimilar to those of a poliovirus circulating in Kuwait in 1977.\nThis current outbreak of rubella seems to be following a similar geographic\njourney, thus far restricted to the same religious subgroup that places\nconstraints on vaccination of its children. Further similarities include\nthe recent declaration of interruption of rubella transmission in the USA\n(see Achievements in public health: elimination of rubella and congenital\nrubella syndrome -- United States, 1969--2004. 2005; 54(early release):1-4,\n21 Mar <http://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/mm54e321a1.htm>). One\nwonders if the virus will follow the same itinerary -- to other neighboring\ngroups that do not vaccinate based on religious beliefs and then south into\nthe USA.\nReturning to rubella, for the individual infected, it is usually a mild\nself-limited febrile rash illness with minimal sequelae. It has been\nassociated with a self-limited poly-arthritis/arthropathy, especially in\nadult women (up to 70 per cent of adult women may manifest this).\nEncephalitis has been reported at a rate of 1 in 6000 infections, again\nmore frequent in adult women. Hemorrhagic manifestations have been reported\nat a rate of 1 in 3000 infections, felt to be due to low platelets and\nvascular damage, more common in children than adults, and most cases\nrecover. The more serious consequences of infection with this virus occur\nwhen a pregnant woman is infected, producing a complex of multiple\ncongenital anomalies in the child, referred to as congenital rubella\nsyndrome. When infection occurs in early gestation, it may lead to fetal\ndeath, spontaneous abortion, or premature delivery. While fetal infection\nmay occur throughout pregnancy, defects are rare when infection occurs\nafter the 20th week of gestation.\nIn the pre-vaccine era, serosurveys conducted in the USA revealed that by\nage 18, about 85 per cent of the population had evidence of prior rubella\ninfection, leaving about 15 per cent of the population over age 18 (and\nthereby in the childbearing age group) susceptible.\nRubella virus was first isolated in 1962 by Parkman and Weller. Rubella\nvirus is classified as a togavirus, genus Rubivirus, and is most closely\nrelated to group A arboviruses, such as Eastern and Western Equine\nEncephalitis viruses. It is an enveloped RNA virus, with a single antigenic\ntype that does not cross-react with other members of the togavirus group.\n(The above background information includes information from the Pink Book\non vaccine preventible diseases chapter on rubella found at:\n<http://www.cdc.gov/nip/publications/pink/rubella.pdf>). - Mod.MPP]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n One\nwonders if the virus will follow the same itinerary -- to other neighboring\ngroups that do not vaccinate based on religious beliefs and then south into\nthe USA.\n The school draws its students\nfrom 4 closely aligned religious denominations: the Heritage Netherlands\nReformed Congregation, the Old Reformed Congregation, the Netherlands\nReformed Congregation of North America, and the Netherlands Reformed\nCongregation. '", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "measles", "poliomyelitis", "rubella"], "syndromes": ["encephalitis"], "event_date": ["1978-03-25 00:00:00", "2010-03-25 00:00:00", "1974-03-25 00:00:00", "2005-05-25 00:00:00", "1977-03-25 00:00:00", "1979-01-25 00:00:00", "1979-07-27 00:00:00", "2021-03-26 00:00:00", "1962-03-25 00:00:00", "2021-03-29 00:00:00", "1969-03-25 00:00:00", "2005-05-10 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Netherlands", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Kingston", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Oxford \n County", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Kuwait", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Canada", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "U.S.", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Ontario", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "the \n Netherlands", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "United States", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "London", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Pennsylvania", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "USA", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "the United States", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2195315", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Rubella - Russia (Don Region)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2195315", "date": "1999-03-20 23:50:00", "main_text": "RUBELLA - RUSSIA (DON REGION)\n*****************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.healthnet.org/programs/promed.html>", "summary": "", "reports": [{"diseases": ["rubella"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": [], "locations": []}]}, {"archive_id": "302903", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Rubella - Bosnia and Herzegovina: Republika Srpska", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=302903", "date": "2009-10-02 03:00:06", "main_text": "RUBELLA - BOSNIA AND HERZEGOVINA: REPUBLIKA SRPSKA\n**************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: 1 Oct 2009\nSource: Eurosurveillance 2009; 14(39) [edited]\n<http://www.eurosurveillance.org/ViewArticle.aspx?ArticleId=19343>\n\n\nOngoing rubella outbreak in Bosnia and Herzegovina, March-July 2009\n-------------------------------------------------------------------\nbyline: A Novo 1, J M Huebschen2, C P Muller2, M Tesanovic3, J Bojanic3.\nAt: 1 WHO Country Office for Bosnia and Herzegovina, Sarajevo, Bosnia and\nHerzegovina;\n2 Institute of Immunology, WHO Collaborating Centre for Reference and\nResearch on Measles Infections, WHO European Regional Reference Laboratory\nfor Measles and Rubella, National Reference Laboratory for Measles and\nRubella, Luxembourg; 3 Public Health Institute Republika Srpska, Banja\nLuka, Bosnia and Herzegovina\n\nBetween 24 Mar and 31 Jul 2009, 342 clinically diagnosed cases of rubella\nwere notified in 5 municipalities in Republika Srpska, Bosnia and\nHerzegovina (BiH). 14 cases were laboratory-confirmed by positive IgG\nagainst rubella virus. 4 virus isolates were obtained and identified as\ngenotype 2B strains, with one isolate differing by a single mutation in the\nE1 gene. This ongoing outbreak revealed gaps in the immunisation programme\nduring the war in BiH (1992-1995) and highlights the need to revise\nlegislation to permit immunisation of children above 14 years of age with\nmeasles, mumps, rubella (MMR) vaccine and to introduce supplemental\nimmunisation activities.\n\nIntroduction\nRubella is a notifiable disease in Bosnia and Herzegovina (BiH; estimated\npopulation 3.9 million) and is reported on the basis of clinical symptoms.\nRubella immunisation was introduced in the 1980s. In 1999-2000, a 2-dose\nschedule with the measles, mumps, rubella (MMR) vaccine was implemented,\nwith the 1st dose given at the age of 12 months (since 2008 at 11 months)\nand the 2nd dose at the age of 7 years and no later than 14 years.\n\nBetween 24 Mar and 31 Jul 2009, 342 clinically diagnosed cases of rubella\nwere notified in 5 municipalities in Republika Srpska (RS), which is one of\n2 governing entities in BiH. At the time of publication of this report, the\noutbreak is ongoing, with circa 4 cases per week. Epidemiological and\nlaboratory investigations were started in early May 2009. Preliminary\nresults are presented below.\n\nMaterials and methods\nSerum samples were collected from 20 suspected rubella cases (6 from\nDubica, 5 from Kotor Varos, 3 from Prijedor, 4 from East Sarajevo-Pale and\n2 from Trebinje). Throat swabs were obtained from the 3 patients from\nPrijedor and from 2 of the 5 patients from Kotor Varos. All sera were\ntested for IgM against measles and rubella and for rubella IgG (Dade\nBehring Enzygnost immunoassays) at the Regional Reference Laboratory (RRL)\nof the World Health Organization Regional Office for Europe (WHO/Europe) in\nLuxembourg, and 10 serum samples were also analysed for rubella IgM at the\nlaboratory of the Public Health Institute of Republika Srpska (PHI RS). The\nthroat swabs were used for PCR analysis as described previously [1] and for\nvirus isolation [2]. Phylogenetic analysis based on the rubella virus E1\nglycoprotein gene was done with MEGA [3], and sequences were compared to\npublished sequences by BLAST.\n\nResults\nOutbreak profile: On 28 May 2009, the PHI RS declared a rubella outbreak in\n3 municipalities in the Banja Luka Region: Prijedor, Dubica and Kotor\nVaros. Later, an outbreak occurred in the East Sarajevo region including\nthe municipalities Pale and Sokolac. In addition, 4 suspected cases were\nreported in Banja Luka and 8 in Doboj.\n\nIn Dubica, 44 rubella cases were reported between 24 Mar and 15 May 2009\n(illustrated graphically in the original text) on the basis of a clinical\ncase definition -- that is, acute onset of generalised maculopapular rash,\nbody temperature higher than 37.2 C and arthralgia/arthritis,\nlymphadenopathy, or conjunctivitis. The outbreak in this area appears to be\nover. The index case was not identified.\n\nIn Kotor Varos, Prijedor and East Sarajevo, where the outbreaks are still\nongoing, 117, 116 and 65 rubella cases, respectively, were reported until\nthe end of July 2009. The last case to date was reported on 15 Sep 2009 in\nSokolac, East Sarajevo.\n\n45 per cent of the cases were male. The age ranged from those born in 1971\nto those born in 2007. Most cases (82 per cent, n=282) were observed among\nteenagers born between 1990 and 1994 still attending high school: 66 per\ncent (29/44) in Dubica, 90 per cent (105/117) in Kotor Varos, all of them\nattending the same school, 87 per cent (101/116) in Prijedor and 72 per\ncent (47/65) in East Sarajevo.\n\nIn Prijedor only 5 of the notified rubella cases had received one dose of\nMMR, while all the other patients were not immunised. The vaccination\nstatus of the cases in Dubica, Kotor Varos and East Sarajevo is still under\ninvestigation.\n\nLaboratory findings\nEight samples were positive for rubella IgM, including 3 from Kotor Varos,\none from Prijedor and 4 from East Sarajevo-Pale, confirming that the\noutbreaks in these regions were caused by rubella. Four sera were\nequivocal, and 8 were IgM-negative for rubella. There was a 100 per cent\nconcordance between the test results obtained at PHI RS and the Luxembourg\nRRL for the 10 sera tested in both laboratories.\n\nThe rubella-positive samples were from 7 patients aged 15 to 17 years and\nfrom one aged 24 years. Five of them had received one dose of rubella\nvaccine, 2 were not vaccinated against rubella, and for one patient no\ninformation on the vaccination status was available. One rubella\nIgM-positive patient was negative for rubella IgG, while the other 7 showed\nrelatively low titres of rubella IgG (less than 77 IU/ml). In total, 14\npatients were positive for rubella IgG, and 6 were negative. All 20 serum\nsamples were negative for measles IgM.\n\nFour of the 5 throat swabs were positive in the diagnostic PCR, and for all\n4 positives, virus isolates were obtained. These samples were collected\nbetween one and 4 days after onset of rash from patients aged 16 or 17\nyears, only one of whom reported having been vaccinated against rubella\nmore than 9 years earlier. Of all 4 PCR-positive samples, nearly complete\nE1 gene sequence data were obtained. Three of the sequences were identical\nfrom Prijedor, and the 4th showed one mutation at position 303 of the E1\ngene and was from Kotor Varos. Phylogenetic analysis attributed the\nsequences to genotype 2B. According to a BLAST analysis, the most similar\npreviously published sequence was an isolate obtained in the United States\n9 years ago (RVi/WA.USA/16.00, GenBank accession number AY968220) with a\nKimura distance of more than 2 per cent.\n\nDiscussion\nThis preliminary report describes a fairly large laboratory-confirmed\noutbreak of rubella affecting mainly unvaccinated or partially vaccinated\nschoolchildren aged 16 to 17 years in 3 contiguous municipalities and one\ndistant region in RS. As the clinical diagnosis of rubella is unreliable,\nthe real number of cases may be somewhat overestimated, as for a few\nsuspected cases, there may have been different reasons for the symptoms\nobserved. This may also explain why some of the sera tested negative for\nrubella IgM. On the other hand, several cases may have remained undetected\ndue to a subclinical course of disease. No cases of rubella were diagnosed\nin BiH in 2008 nor in January and February 2009.\n\nDue to lack of laboratory confirmation, the outbreak was recognised in the\n1st community (Dubica) on 24 Mar 2009. The index case was not identified,\nand, therefore, it is not clear when and from where the virus was\nintroduced. As the most similar published sequence was found in the United\nStates in 2000 and the genetic distance to that isolate was more than 2 per\ncent, the origin of the virus remains obscure.\n\nIn early April 2009, the 1st cases were observed in 2 other municipalities,\nKotor Varos and Prijedor, and in June 2009 in another 2 located 250-400 km\naway. In all of these areas, the epidemic is ongoing. Local epidemiologists\nspeculate that the virus may have spread among teenagers during their stay\nin Mrakovica, Kozara mountain (56 km south from Dubica), which is a very\npopular place for regular school excursions in spring.\n\nTo date, there is no information on occurrence of rubella in pregnant women\nor abortion in connection with the current rubella outbreak. Due to the\nrisk of congenital rubella infection during the 1st trimester of pregnancy,\nwhich can lead to miscarriage, stillbirth, or infants with birth defects,\nrubella is of high public health importance.\n\nBefore the war in 1990, coverage with MMR vaccine was 93.6 per cent in BiH.\nVaccine procurement and implementation of the immunisation programme were\ndifficult during the war, and in the last 2 years of war, MMR vaccine\ncoverage was only 56.8 per cent. The age groups primarily affected in the\ncurrent outbreak were born during the war, and most of them were not even\nvaccinated with the 1st dose of MMR. Surveys done in RS in 1999 and in 2006\nshowed MMR vaccination coverage rates of only 54 per cent and 79 per cent,\nrespectively, among children aged 12 to 23 months [4]. Annual statistics\nfrom PHI RS show varying vaccination coverage rates in recent years (2006:\n1st dose 83 per cent, 2nd dose 83 per cent, 2007: 92 per cent and 93 per\ncent, 2008: 78 per cent and 52 per cent), indicating that other age groups\nmay also contain people at risk for infection.\n\nAs a result of the outbreaks, the Minister of Health and Social Welfare and\nthe PHI RS have initiated immediate actions to improve the coverage with\nthe 2nd dose of MMR vaccine in children under the age of 14 years and have\nalerted the Regional Public Health Institutes and primary health care\nproviders of the emerging outbreak. An action plan to initiate\nsupplementary immunisation of children and young adults with measles and\nrubella vaccine or rubella vaccine is presently being developed with\nsupport from WHO/Europe. The ongoing rubella outbreak also highlights the\nneed for a revised legislation that permits MMR vaccination in children\nolder than 14 years as well as the need to improve the surveillance of\ncongenital rubella syndrome.\n\nReferences\n1. The Americas", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=123474", "date": "2008-10-30 23:00:34", "main_text": "RUBELLA, 2003-2008 - THE AMERICAS\n******************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Fri 31 Oct 2008\nSource: MMWR Weekly 2008/57(43);1176-1179 [edited]\n<http://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/mm5743a4.htm?s_cid=mm5743a4_e>\n\n\nProgress Toward Elimination of Rubella and Congenital Rubella\nSyndrome -- the Americas, 2003--2008\n-------------------------------------------\nIn 2003, the Pan American Health Organization (PAHO) adopted a\nresolution calling for rubella and congenital rubella syndrome (CRS)\nelimination in the Americas by the year 2010 (1). Elimination was\ndefined as the interruption of endemic rubella virus transmission in\nall countries of North America, Central America, South America, and\nthe Caribbean for more than 12 months and no occurrence of CRS cases\nattributed to endemic transmission (2). To accomplish this goal, PAHO\ndeveloped a rubella and CRS elimination strategy (3) to 1) introduce\nrubella-containing vaccine (RCV) into routine vaccination programs of\nall countries for children aged 12 months and reach greater than 95\npercent coverage in all municipalities, 2) conduct a one-time mass\ncampaign among adolescents and adults and periodic follow-up\ncampaigns among children aged less than 5 years (4), and 3) integrate\nrubella surveillance with measles surveillance and initiate CRS\nsurveillance. During 1998-2006, confirmed rubella cases decreased 98\npercent (from 135 947 to 2998) in the Americas. However, in 2007,\nrubella outbreaks with a total of 13 014 cases occurred in 3\ncountries (Argentina, Brazil, and Chile), primarily in males not\nincluded in previous vaccination campaigns. This report summarizes\noverall progress toward reaching the 2010 goal of eliminating rubella\nand CRS. With completion of campaigns in Argentina, Brazil, and\nHaiti, all countries will have implemented the recommended PAHO\nstrategy by the end of 2008, with the expectation of reaching the\n2010 rubella and CRS elimination goal.\n\nRoutine Vaccination\n--------------------\nAll 38 countries and territories in the Americas, with the exception\nof Haiti, have introduced measles-mumps-rubella vaccine (MMR) in\ntheir routine immunization schedules. Beginning in 2009, Haiti will\nintroduce measles-rubella (MR) vaccine in its routine immunization\nprogram, after completion of a one-time MR mass vaccination campaign.\n\nIn the Americas, routine MMR vaccination coverage is calculated by\ncountries in one of 3 ways: based on the number of doses administered\n(34 countries), nominal registries (Mexico and Uruguay), and coverage\nsurveys (Canada and the United States). Reported regional routine 1st\ndose MMR (MMR1) coverage at age 12 months was 93-94 percent during\n2003-2007. In 2007, MMR1 coverage of greater than 95 percent was\nreported in 19 (51 percent) countries, 90-94 percent in 7 (19\npercent) countries, 80-89 percent in 7 (19 percent) countries, and\nless than 80 percent in 4 (11 percent) countries. In 2007, of the 20\ncountries and territories reporting administration of a 2nd routine\nMMR dose (MMR2), reported coverage was greater than 95 percent in 3\n(15 percent) countries, 90-94 percent in 3 (15 percent) countries,\n80-89 percent in 8 (40 percent) countries, and less than 80 percent\nin 6 (30 percent) countries.\n\nSupplementary Immunization Activities\n-------------------------------------\nAs part of the PAHO rubella and CRS elimination strategy, 2 different\ntypes of supplementary immunization activities (SIAs) are\nrecommended: one-time SIAs targeting all adolescents and adults, and\nperiodic follow-up SIAs usually targeting children aged 1-4 years.\nOne-time SIAs targeting all adolescents and adults are intended to\nquickly interrupt rubella virus transmission and prevent future CRS\ncases (5). Periodic follow-up campaigns provide a 2nd opportunity to\nvaccinate children who were never vaccinated or who failed to develop\nan immune response.\n\nDuring 1998-2008, approximately 250 million adolescents and adults in\n32 countries and territories were vaccinated against rubella in SIAs.\nThree countries initially only vaccinated females during SIAs: Chile\nvaccinated females aged 10-29 years in 1999, Brazil vaccinated\nfemales aged 12-39 years during 2001-2002, and Argentina vaccinated\nfemales aged 18-39 years in 2006. After those SIAs, transmission and\noutbreaks of rubella mainly occurred among adolescent and adult males\nin the 3 countries. Subsequent SIAs were conducted during 2007 and\n2008 in Chile (1.3 million males aged 19-29 years in 2007), Brazil\n(70 million males and females aged 20-39 years and 12-39 years in 5\nselected states in 2008), and Argentina (6.5 million males aged 16-39\nyears in 2008). Aruba and Netherlands Antilles, Canada, the French\ndepartments (French Guiana, Guadeloupe, and Martinique), Panama, the\nUnited States, and Uruguay introduced RCV in the routine childhood\nschedule greater than 20 years ago, thus protecting large cohorts of\nthe adult population. As a result, these countries decided not to\nimplement adolescent and adult SIAs. All Latin American and Caribbean\ncountries that have introduced RCV since 1995 have implemented at\nleast one follow-up campaign.\n\nSurveillance Activities\n------------------------\nCase-based measles and rubella surveillance is carried out in all\ncountries of the Americas and reported weekly to the regional level.\nAll public and private health-care providers are required to report\nsuspected measles and rubella cases; however, reporting is\nincomplete, particularly in the private sector. Since 1996, serum\nspecimens from patients with suspected measles testing negative for\nmeasles immunoglobulin M (IgM) antibody have been tested for\nrubella-specific IgM antibody. In 1999, regional rubella surveillance\nwas integrated with existing case-based measles surveillance in the\nPAHO regional measles and rubella laboratory network of 21 national\nand 124 sub-national laboratories. Within the integrated measles and\nrubella surveillance system, measles or rubella cases are counted as\nsuspected when a health-care worker suspects that a patient has\nmeasles or rubella infection. Rubella cases are counted as confirmed\nwhen a patient has laboratory-confirmed rubella (IgM positive) or an\ninfection that meets the clinical case definition and is linked\nepidemiologically to a laboratory-confirmed case. A suspected CRS\ncase is defined in any infant whose mother had laboratory-confirmed\nrubella infection during pregnancy or in any infant who has\ncongenital anomalies compatible with CRS. A confirmed CRS case is\ndefined as infection in a child with compatible birth defects and\ndocumented laboratory evidence of rubella infection during the 1st\nyear of life (6).\n\nDuring 1998-2006, confirmed rubella cases decreased 98 percent, from\n135 947 to 2998 (data illustrated by a figure in the original text).\nA shift in distribution and increase in cases in 2007 resulted from\noutbreaks, particularly among males, in Argentina (96 cases), Brazil\n(8683 cases), and Chile (4235 cases), countries that initially\nvaccinated only females in rubella SIAs. During 1 Jan-20 Sep 2008, a\ntotal of 2039 confirmed rubella cases were reported in the PAHO\nregion, of which Argentina, Brazil, and Chile accounted for 98\npercent. In response to these outbreaks, countries intensified\nsurveillance activities and vaccination interventions by conducting\nSIAs among adolescents and adults. Countries that have completed SIAs\nfor all adolescents and adults have not reported any endemic rubella\ncases [These data are tabulated in the original text.].\n\nIn 2007, 975 suspected CRS cases were reported in the 34 countries\nwith CRS surveillance; 4 countries (Brazil, Chile, Colombia, and\nPeru) accounted for 947 (97 percent) of these cases. 19 cases were\nconfirmed by detection of rubella IgM antibody, 17 from Brazil and 2\nfrom Peru. During 1 Jan-20 Sep 2008, confirmed CRS cases were\nreported in Argentina (one), Brazil (3), and Chile (2).\n\nDuring 2003-2007, wild-type rubella viruses of genotypes 1C and 2B\nwere endemic in the Americas. Viruses of genotypes 1E, 1G, 1j, and 2B\nhave been linked to imported cases.\n\nPAHO recommends the use of performance indicators to measure measles\nand rubella surveillance quality. The current standardized\nsurveillance indicators and targets recommended in countries of the\nAmericas to monitor progress toward rubella elimination include: 1)\nweekly notification by 80 percent of reporting units (i.e., health\nclinics, usually at least one for each municipality), 2) greater than\n80 percent of suspected measles/rubella cases investigated\nadequately, 3) 2 or more suspected cases of measles or rubella per\n100 000 persons detected and reported (to monitor the sensitivity of\nthe surveillance) and 4) greater than 80 percent of suspected\nmeasles/rubella cases with serologic testing. In 2007, among 34\nreporting countries and territories in the region, these respective\ntargets were met by 88 percent, 72 percent, 71 percent, and 85\npercent of countries. Aruba and Netherlands Antilles, Canada, the\nFrench departments, and the United States do not report indicator\ndata regularly.\n\nBeginning in 2009, 2 additional indicators and targets will be\nmonitored by countries and PAHO: 1) greater than 80 percent of\nconfirmed cases are accompanied by follow-up of contacts for 30 days\n(to monitor occurrence of secondary cases); and 2) greater than 80\npercent of outbreaks have adequate specimens collected for virus\ndetection and isolation.\n\n[Reported by: C Castillo-Solorzano, MD, C Marsigli, MPH, P Bravo\nAlcantara, MPH, JK Andrus, MD, AMB Filippis, PhD, MC Danovaro-\nHolliday, MD, C Ruiz Matus, MD, Comprehensive Family Immunization\nProject, Pan American Health Organization, Washington, DC. Ministries\nof health or public health authorities in the countries of the\nRegions of the Americas. S Reef, MD, SL Cochi, MD, Global\nImmunization Div, National Center for Immunization and Respiratory\nDiseases, CDC.]\n\nMMWR Editorial Note\n----------------------\nIn response to ongoing rubella circulation and the potential for\nmajor epidemics, PAHO developed a comprehensive strategy in 2004 to\neliminate rubella and CRS from the Americas by 2010. Countries have\ndemonstrated progress toward the rubella and CRS elimination goal;\nhowever, outbreaks have occurred, primarily among males, in countries\nthat only vaccinated females during mass campaigns. By implementing\nthe comprehensive strategy and including males in SIAs, countries are\nable to limit or prevent future outbreaks. By the end of 2008, all\ncountries and territories of the Americas will have implemented the\nPAHO-recommended vaccination strategies.\n\nIn 2005, the United States was the 1st country in the Americas to\ndeclare it had eliminated endemic rubella virus transmission (7). In\naccordance with the PAHO biennial work plan, 20 additional countries\nare expected to begin the process of verifying interruption of\nrubella virus transmission in 2009. To prepare for verification of\nelimination of rubella and CRS in the Americas, PAHO is developing a\nstrategic plan to guide countries on how to document elimination. In\naddition, in 2007, the 27th Pan American Sanitary Conference approved\na resolution that defined the final steps for reaching the rubella\nelimination goal by 2010, including formation of national commissions\nto compile and analyze data and an international committee to\ndocument progress toward interrupting transmission (8).\n\nAs the final stage of rubella elimination approaches, several\nchallenges remain, including the continued risk for rubella\nimportations and limited collection of specimens for virus detection\nand isolation. To confront the challenges, PAHO has been working with\ncountries to 1) maintain high population immunity through high\nroutine vaccination coverage and completion of high-quality SIAs; 2)\nmaintain high-quality integrated measles-rubella surveillance and CRS\nsurveillance, including distributing practical field guides,\nmonitoring confirmed CRS cases for virus excretion with at least 2\nconsecutive negative specimens, and improving the participation of\nclinicians in the private sector in surveillance activities; 3)\nstrengthen the WHO regional measles and rubella laboratory network in\nthe Americas, including emphasis on obtaining specimens for\ngenotyping; and 4) increase training opportunities for health workers\nand interdisciplinary teams of epidemiologists, virologists, and\nclinicians.\n\nReferences\n--------\n(1) Pan American Health Organization. In 2007, of the 20\ncountries and territories reporting administration of a 2nd routine\nMMR dose (MMR2), reported coverage was greater than 95 percent in 3\n(15 percent) countries, 90-94 percent in 3 (15 percent) countries,\n80-89 percent in 8 (40 percent) countries, and less than 80 percent\nin 6 (30 percent) countries. Beginning in 2009, 2 additional indicators and targets will be\nmonitored by countries and PAHO: 1) greater than 80 percent of\nconfirmed cases are accompanied by follow-up of contacts for 30 days\n(to monitor occurrence of secondary cases); and 2) greater than 80\npercent of outbreaks have adequate specimens collected for virus\ndetection and isolation.\n\n In\naddition, in 2007, the 27th Pan American Sanitary Conference approved\na resolution that defined the final steps for reaching the rubella\nelimination goal by 2010, including formation of national commissions\nto compile and analyze data and an international committee to\ndocument progress toward interrupting transmission (8).\n\n A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Fri 31 Oct 2008\n The groups of territories\ninclude 3 French departments (French Guiana, Guadeloupe, and\nMartinique), the autonomous region of the Kingdom of the Netherlands\n(Aruba and Netherlands Antilles), and 6 United Kingdom overseas\nterritories (Anguilla, Bermuda, British Virgin Islands, Cayman\nIslands, Montserrat, and Turks and Caicos Islands). In 2007, MMR1 coverage of greater than 95 percent was\nreported in 19 (51 percent) countries, 90-94 percent in 7 (19\npercent) countries, 80-89 percent in 7 (19 percent) countries, and\nless than 80 percent in 4 (11 percent) countries. Subsequent SIAs were conducted during 2007 and\n2008 in Chile (1.3 million males aged 19-29 years in 2007), Brazil\n(70 million males and females aged 20-39 years and 12-39 years in 5\nselected states in 2008), and Argentina (6.5 million males aged 16-39\nyears in 2008). Elimination was\ndefined as the interruption of endemic rubella virus transmission in\nall countries of North America, Central America, South America, and\nthe Caribbean for more than 12 months and no occurrence of CRS cases\nattributed to endemic transmission (2). Within the integrated measles and\nrubella surveillance system, measles or rubella cases are counted as\nsuspected when a health-care worker suspects that a patient has\nmeasles or rubella infection. Aruba and Netherlands Antilles, Canada, the French\ndepartments (French Guiana, Guadeloupe, and Martinique), Panama, the\nUnited States, and Uruguay introduced RCV in the routine childhood\nschedule greater than 20 years ago, thus protecting large cohorts of\nthe adult population. A shift in distribution and increase in cases in 2007 resulted from\noutbreaks, particularly among males, in Argentina (96 cases), Brazil\n(8683 cases), and Chile (4235 cases), countries that initially\nvaccinated only females in rubella SIAs. In 2007, among 34\nreporting countries and territories in the region, these respective\ntargets were met by 88 percent, 72 percent, 71 percent, and 85\npercent of countries. In 1999, regional rubella surveillance\nwas integrated with existing case-based measles surveillance in the\nPAHO regional measles and rubella laboratory network of 21 national\nand 124 sub-national laboratories. Rubella cases are counted as confirmed\nwhen a patient has laboratory-confirmed rubella (IgM positive) or an\ninfection that meets the clinical case definition and is linked\nepidemiologically to a laboratory-confirmed case. Countries have\ndemonstrated progress toward the rubella and CRS elimination goal;\nhowever, outbreaks have occurred, primarily among males, in countries\nthat only vaccinated females during mass campaigns. However, in 2007,\nrubella outbreaks with a total of 13 014 cases occurred in 3\ncountries (Argentina, Brazil, and Chile), primarily in males not\nincluded in previous vaccination campaigns. Three countries initially only vaccinated females during SIAs: Chile\nvaccinated females aged 10-29 years in 1999, Brazil vaccinated\nfemales aged 12-39 years during 2001-2002, and Argentina vaccinated\nfemales aged 18-39 years in 2006. As part of the PAHO rubella and CRS elimination strategy, 2 different\ntypes of supplementary immunization activities (SIAs) are\nrecommended: one-time SIAs targeting all adolescents and adults, and\nperiodic follow-up SIAs usually targeting children aged 1-4 years. As the final stage of rubella elimination approaches, several\nchallenges remain, including the continued risk for rubella\nimportations and limited collection of specimens for virus detection\nand isolation. Source: MMWR Weekly 2008/57(43);1176-1179 [edited]\n<http://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/mm5743a4.htm?s_cid=mm5743a4_e>\n\n\nProgress Toward Elimination of Rubella and Congenital Rubella\nSyndrome -- the Americas, 2003--2008\n During 1 Jan-20 Sep 2008, a\ntotal of 2039 confirmed rubella cases were reported in the PAHO\nregion, of which Argentina, Brazil, and Chile accounted for 98\npercent. With completion of campaigns in Argentina, Brazil, and\nHaiti, all countries will have implemented the recommended PAHO\nstrategy by the end of 2008, with the expectation of reaching the\n2010 rubella and CRS elimination goal. In\naccordance with the PAHO biennial work plan, 20 additional countries\nare expected to begin the process of verifying interruption of\nrubella virus transmission in 2009. In the Americas, routine MMR vaccination coverage is calculated by\ncountries in one of 3 ways: based on the number of doses administered\n(34 countries), nominal registries (Mexico and Uruguay), and coverage\nsurveys (Canada and the United States). Since 1996, serum\nspecimens from patients with suspected measles testing negative for\nmeasles immunoglobulin M (IgM) antibody have been tested for\nrubella-specific IgM antibody. A suspected CRS\ncase is defined in any infant whose mother had laboratory-confirmed\nrubella infection during pregnancy or in any infant who has\ncongenital anomalies compatible with CRS. A confirmed CRS case is\ndefined as infection in a child with compatible birth defects and\ndocumented laboratory evidence of rubella infection during the 1st\nyear of life (6). Communicated by:\nProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[The Americas includes 35 PAHO member states and 3 participating\nstates (France, The Netherlands, and the United Kingdom) with\naffiliated territories in the Americas. S Reef, MD, SL Cochi, MD, Global\nImmunization Div, National Center for Immunization and Respiratory\nDiseases, CDC.]\n\nMMWR Editorial Note\n \n\nIn 2007, 975 suspected CRS cases were reported in the 34 countries\nwith CRS surveillance; 4 countries (Brazil, Chile, Colombia, and\nPeru) accounted for 947 (97 percent) of these cases. In 2003, the Pan American Health Organization (PAHO) adopted a\nresolution calling for rubella and congenital rubella syndrome (CRS)\nelimination in the Americas by the year 2010 (1). Rubella and congenital rubella\nsyndrome elimination strategy: contributing to primary health care\nrenewal. Countries that have completed SIAs\nfor all adolescents and adults have not reported any endemic rubella\ncases [These data are tabulated in the original text.]. ProMED-mail readers with specific\nknowledge of rubella activity/outbreaks in Mexico or Central America\nare requested to e-mail Ms. Laura Tabony, TDH Immunization Division,\nat: <laura.tabony@tdh.state.tx.us> **********************\nThe Immunization Division, Texas Department of Health (TDH), in\nconjunction with the US Immigration and Naturalization Service (INS),\nhas investigated an outbreak of rubella among detainees (from Mexico\nand Central America) at the INS Detention Facility in Port Isabel,\nCameron County, Texas (southern-most county in Texas, borders\nTamaulipas State, Mexico). Rubella has been rare in Texas, with only\neight (8) cases reported in each of 1995 and 1996, for a rate/100,000\nof < 0.1 cases/100,000 population; Texas' population is roughly 19\nmillion.\nA TDH 'Rubella Alert!' dated March 30, 1998, states in part:\n'Twenty-one cases of rubella have been confirmed in a federal\nImmigration & Naturalization Detention Facility in Port Isabel,\nTexas...The first case occurred on November 30, 1997, and the most\nrecent experienced rash onset on March 26, 1998. Twenty (20) cases\nare detainees of Hispanic ethnicity who came from El Salvador (5),\nHonduras (7), Guatemala (5), and Mexico (3). They ranged in age from\n18 years to 31 years; median age was 20 years. One case was an\nunvaccinated employee working in the clinic. In addition to the 21\ncases that have been confirmed within this facility, three cases have\noccurred in neighboring communities-one case in Brownsville (Cameron\nCo) and two cases in San Juan (Hidalgo Co)...Furthermore, outbreaks\nof rubella are known to be ongoing in several Mexican states...'\nWith the rapidly approaching Easter/Semana Santa holiday, large\nnumbers of people are expected to cross the border between Texas (and\nother US border states) and Mexico, visiting family, etc. throughout\nMexico (and perhaps countries in Central America), and subsequently\nreturning to Texas/the US.\n--\nJames N.
